10037{_}933{_}Sometimes she would sit for hours with the stately cat upon her knee, thinking, thinking, thinking, while Pompey sang his favorite hymns about his work and the mellow strains floated up the stairway and soothed her lonely heart.
10037{_}934{_}His childlike faith became to her a tower of refuge, and often, when bewildered by life's inconsistencies, she felt as if the eternal realities were vanishing into mist, she was calmed and comforted by his happy trust.
10037{_}935{_}"I cannot imagine, Evadne," said Isabelle one evening at dinner, "what pleasure you can find in sitting in a stable in company with a negro!
10037{_}936{_}It certainly shows a most depraved taste."
10037{_}937{_}"Christ was born in a stable, Isabelle."
10037{_}938{_}"What in the world has that to do with you?"
10037{_}939{_}"I am beginning to think he has everything to do with me," answered her cousin quietly.
10037{_}940{_}"Well," said Isabelle with a toss of her head, "we are known by the company we keep.
10037{_}941{_}I should imagine Pompey's curriculum of manners was not on a very elevated plane."
10037{_}942{_}"Pompey!
10037{_}943{_}Isabelle," said Judge Hildreth suddenly.
10037{_}944{_}"Why, my dear, Pompey is a modern Socrates, bound in ebony.
10037{_}945{_}There is no danger to be apprehended from him."
10037{_}946{_}"Well, it is a peculiar companionship for Judge Hildreth's niece, that is all I have to say," said Isabelle coldly, "but _chacun a son gout_."
10037{_}947{_}"I read this morning in your Bible that God had chosen the base things of the world, and things which are despised, and things which are not, to bring to nought things that are.
10037{_}948{_}What does that mean, Isabelle?"
10037{_}949{_}"Really, Evadne, we shall have to send you to live with Doctor Jerome!"
10037{_}950{_}said her aunt, with a careless laugh.
10037{_}951{_}"You are getting to be a regular interrogation point.
10037{_}952{_}We are not Bible commentators, child, you cannot expect us to explain all the difficult passages.
10037{_}953{_}"The Embroidery Club meets here tomorrow, Evadne," exclaimed Marion, "and I don't believe you have touched your table scarf since they were here before.
10037{_}954{_}What will Celeste Follingsby think?
10037{_}955{_}She works so rapidly, and her drawn work is a perfect poem."
10037{_}956{_}"No, I have not," confessed Evadne.
10037{_}957{_}"It seems such silly work, to draw threads apart and then sew them together again."
10037{_}958{_}Isabelle elevated her eyebrows with a look of horror.
10037{_}959{_}Louis laughed.
10037{_}960{_}"She's a hopeless case, Isabelle.
10037{_}961{_}You'll never convert her into an elegant trifler.
10037{_}962{_}You might as well throw up the contract."
10037{_}963{_}"It seems to me, Evadne," said his sister icily, "that you might have a little regard for the decorums of society.
10037{_}964{_}Don't, I beg of you, give utterance to such heresies before the girls.
10037{_}965{_}And I wish you would not call it _my_ Bible.
10037{_}966{_}I did not make it."
10037{_}967{_}"That is quite true, Evadne," said Louis gravely.
10037{_}968{_}"If she had, there would have been a good deal left out."
10037{_}969{_}Isabella shot an angry glance at him but made no remark.
10037{_}970{_}Her brother's sarcasms were always received in silence.
10037{_}971{_}"Eva," she said after a pause, "I intend to call you by that name in future,--your full one is too troublesome."
10037{_}972{_}Evadne shivered.
10037{_}973{_}Her father was the only one who had ever abbreviated her name.
10037{_}974{_}"I shall not answer to it," she said quietly.
10037{_}975{_}"Why, pray?"
10037{_}976{_}"Because, I suppose, in common with the rest of the lower animals, I have a natural repugnance to being cut in two."
10037{_}977{_}"How tiresome you are!"
10037{_}978{_}exclaimed Isabelle with a pout.
10037{_}979{_}"I do not object to my first syllable.
10037{_}980{_}All the girls at school call me Isa.
10037{_}981{_}Mamma, did you remember to order the tulle for our wings?
10037{_}982{_}Claude Rivers has finished hers and they are perfectly sweet.
10037{_}983{_}She showed them to me this afternoon."
10037{_}984{_}"Wings, Isabelle!
10037{_}985{_}What in the world are you up to now?"
10037{_}986{_}"A Butterfly Social, Papa.
10037{_}987{_}We must raise money in some way.
10037{_}988{_}The church is frightfully in debt."
10037{_}989{_}"That is a deplorable fact, but I did not know butterflies were famed as financiers."
10037{_}990{_}"Oh, of course it is just for the novelty of the thing.
10037{_}991{_}The last social we had was a Mother Goose, and we have had Brownie suppers and Pink teas and everything else we could think of.
10037{_}992{_}We must have something to attract, you know."
10037{_}993{_}"I wonder if it really pays?"
10037{_}994{_}ventured Marion.
10037{_}995{_}"It never seems to me there is much left, after you deduct the cost of the preparation.
10037{_}996{_}People might as well give the money outright.
10037{_}997{_}It would save them a world of trouble."
10037{_}998{_}"Why, you silly child, it is to promote sociability in the church.
10037{_}999{_}As to the trouble, of course we do not count that.
10037{_}1000{_}We must expect to make sacrifices."
10037{_}1001{_}"But they do not make the church any more sociable," said Marion boldly, who, having struck for freedom of thought, was following up her advantage.
10037{_}1002{_}"The same people take part every time and the others are left outside."
10037{_}1003{_}"Nonsense!"
10037{_}1004{_}said Isabelle hotly.
10037{_}1005{_}"It is only those who cannot afford to take part, and think what a treat it is for them to look on!"
10037{_}1006{_}"A sort of half-price theatre," said Louis with a sneer.
10037{_}1007{_}"I don't believe they find the looking on such fun as you think," said Marion, who was astonished at herself.
10037{_}1008{_}"Suppose you try if they wouldn't like to take part and offer your place in the Cantata to Jemima Dobbs."
10037{_}1009{_}"Well done, Sis!"
10037{_}1010{_}and Louis applauded softly.
10037{_}1011{_}Isabelle's lip curled.
10037{_}1012{_}"Upon my word, Marion, you bid fair to become as hot an anarchist as Louise Michel.
10037{_}1013{_}It is a mystery to me where you find out the Christian names of all the ungainly people in the congregation.
10037{_}1014{_}The other sopranos would feel complimented to have a prima-donna with a face like a full moon and hands like a blacksmith's foisted upon them!
10037{_}1015{_}One must have a little regard for appearances," and Isabelle drew her graceful figure up to its full height.
10037{_}1016{_}"Jemima Dobbs isn't dynamite, and I have no anarchical tendencies," persisted Marion stoutly,--"but beauty is only skin deep, Isabelle.
10037{_}1017{_}She supports a sick mother and five children and that is more than any of the rest of us could do," and Marion, frightened at her momentary temerity, shrank back into her shell.
10037{_}1018{_}"It is a most unaccountable thing, Lawrence," said Mrs. Hildreth, "why the church should be so heavily encumbered.
10037{_}1019{_}I am sure you contribute handsomely and the pew rents are high.
10037{_}1020{_}There is always a large congregation.
10037{_}1021{_}I cannot understand."
10037{_}1022{_}"It is largely composed of transients though, my dear, and they never carry more than a nickel in their pockets, so the weight of the burden falls upon a few.
10037{_}1023{_}The expenses are very heavy.
10037{_}1024{_}Jerome wants to make it the most popular church in the city, and the new quartette proves an extravagant luxury."
10037{_}1025{_}"Oh, well," said Mrs. Hildreth, "of course one cannot grudge the money for that.
10037{_}1026{_}Professional singing is such an attraction!
10037{_}1027{_}The way Madame Rialto took that high C last Sunday was superb."
10037{_}1028{_}"Well," said Isabelle, "I don't think there is any doubt that Doctor Jerome is the most popular preacher in the city.
10037{_}1029{_}He is going to preach next Sunday on the moral progress of social sciences, and next month he commences his series of sermons on the social problems of the day.
10037{_}1030{_}He does take such an interest in sociology."
10037{_}1031{_}"But why doesn't he preach Jesus Christ?"
10037{_}1032{_}asked Evadne wonderingly.
10037{_}1033{_}"You will get to be a regular fanatic, Evadne, if you ring the changes on that subject so often.
10037{_}1034{_}Doctor Jerome says he wants his people to have an intelligent idea of the progress of events.
10037{_}1035{_}Of course everyone understands the Bible.
10037{_}1036{_}"I do think he is the loveliest man!"
10037{_}1037{_}she continued rapturously, "he is so sympathetic; and Celeste Follingsby says he is 'perfectly heavenly in affliction.'
10037{_}1038{_}Her little sister died last week, you know.
10037{_}1039{_}It is so awkward that it should have happened just now.
10037{_}1040{_}She will not be able to take any part in the Cantata, and she had the sweetest dress!"
10037{_}1041{_}"Very ill-timed of Providence!"
10037{_}1042{_}said Louis gravely.
10037{_}1043{_}"What a pity it is, Isabelle, that you couldn't have the regulation of affairs."
10037{_}1044{_}He yawned and strolled lazily towards the fireplace.
10037{_}1045{_}When he looked round again, Evadne was the only other occupant of the room.
10037{_}1046{_}"Well, coz, what do you think of the situation?
10037{_}1047{_}I belong to the worldlings, of course, but I confess the idea of Jesus Christ at a Butterfly Social is tremendously incongruous.
10037{_}1048{_}We have the best of it, Evadne, for we live up to our theories.
10037{_}1049{_}Give it up, coz.
10037{_}1050{_}You'll find it a hopeless task to make the Bible and modern Christianity agree."
10037{_}1051{_}He looked at his watch.
10037{_}1052{_}"I say, Evadne, Jefferson is playing at the Metropolitan in Richard III.
10037{_}1053{_}to-night.
10037{_}1054{_}Let us go and hear him."
10037{_}1055{_}And Evadne went, and enjoyed it immensely.
10037{_}1056{_}CHAPTER VII.
10037{_}1057{_}"I am going for a long ride into the country, Evadne," said her uncle one morning, "would you like to come with me?"
10037{_}1058{_}Evadne gave a glad assent.
10037{_}1059{_}After her beautiful tropical life, it seemed to her as if she should choke, shut away from the wide expanse of sky which she loved, among monotonous rows of houses and dingy streets.
10037{_}1060{_}As they left the city behind them and the road swept out into the open, she gave a long sigh of delight.
10037{_}1061{_}Her uncle laughed.
10037{_}1062{_}"Well, Evadne, does it please you?"
10037{_}1063{_}"It is the first time I have felt as if I could breathe," she said.
10037{_}1064{_}"So you don't take kindly to Marlborough?
10037{_}1065{_}Well, I suppose it is a rude awakening from your sunny land, but you will get used to it.
10037{_}1066{_}We grow accustomed to all life's disagreeable surprises as time rolls on."
10037{_}1067{_}Evadne shivered.
10037{_}1068{_}"I do not think I shall ever grow accustomed to it, Uncle Lawrence."
10037{_}1069{_}"Ah, you are young.
10037{_}1070{_}We grow wiser as our hair turns grey."
10037{_}1071{_}"If that is wisdom, I do not care to grow wise."
10037{_}1072{_}"Not grow wise, Evadne!"
10037{_}1073{_}said her uncle quizzically.
10037{_}1074{_}"In this age, when women claim a surplusage of all the brain power bestowed upon the race!
10037{_}1075{_}What will you do when you have to attend to business?"
10037{_}1076{_}"Business," echoed Evadne, "I have never thought about it, Uncle Lawrence."
10037{_}1077{_}"No turn for dollars and cents, eh?
10037{_}1078{_}Did your father never consult you about his affairs?"
10037{_}1079{_}Evadne's lip quivered.
10037{_}1080{_}"Oh, yes," she said, and her words were a cry of pain, "he consulted me about everything, but I do not think there was ever any mention of money.
10037{_}1081{_}Does money constitute business, Uncle Lawrence?"
10037{_}1082{_}"Wealth gives power, Evadne.
10037{_}1083{_}Money is one of the greatest things in the world.
10037{_}1084{_}While we are on the subject I may as well tell you that your father wrote me concerning the disposition of his property.
10037{_}1085{_}I shall look after your interests carefully, together with my own, and give you the same quarterly allowance that my own girls have.
10037{_}1086{_}When you are older I will go more into detail, but it is not worth while now to worry your head over columns of uninteresting figures.
10037{_}1087{_}I shall open an account for you at the National Bank and you can draw on that for your expenses.
10037{_}1088{_}Your aunt will initiate you into the mysteries of shopping.
10037{_}1089{_}By the way, you must have gone through that experience in Barbadoes.
10037{_}1090{_}How did you manage there?"
10037{_}1091{_}Evadne turned her head away and clenched her hands tightly as the flood of bitter-sweet memories threatened to engulf her.
10037{_}1092{_}"Papa always went with me," she said slowly, "whatever he liked I chose."
10037{_}1093{_}Judge Hildreth gave a sigh of relief.
10037{_}1094{_}He had extricated himself from a difficult position with diplomatic skill.
10037{_}1095{_}It did not occur to him that a lie which is half the truth is the meanest kind of a lie.
10037{_}1096{_}He had acquainted his niece with all that was necessary for her to know at present, and at the same time left himself a loophole of escape from the imputation of disregarding his brother's wishes.
10037{_}1097{_}When she became old enough to assume the responsibility, and he got his affairs straightened out sufficiently to admit of transferring to her care the funds which were so absolutely essential to his present success, he would put Evadne in full possession of her inheritance.
10037{_}1098{_}Results had proved the wisdom of his decision.
10037{_}1099{_}By her own acknowledgment his niece had never given a thought to the subject.
10037{_}1100{_}His brother's plan would be a height of imprudence from which he was bound to shield her.
10037{_}1101{_}In Evadne's mind also thought was busy.
10037{_}1102{_}"Money is one of the greatest things in the world," her uncle had said, and she had read that morning, "tongues shall cease, and knowledge shall be done away, but love never faileth.
10037{_}1103{_}Now abideth faith, hope, and love; the greatest of these is love."
10037{_}1104{_}Was Louis right?
10037{_}1105{_}Did Christians and the Bible not agree?
10037{_}1106{_}And the business of _her_ life was to find Jesus Christ.
10037{_}1107{_}Was there any money in that?
10037{_}1108{_}When they reached Hollywood, where Judge Hildreth had business with Mr. Hawthorne, Evadne was in an ecstasy of silent rapture.
10037{_}1109{_}She had never dreamed what a New England farm might be.
10037{_}1110{_}Its varied beauty, clad in the dazzling robes of early summer, came upon her with the suddenness of a revelation.
10037{_}1111{_}She begged to be allowed to wait for her uncle out of doors, and wandered slowly on past the great barns to where the wide gate stretched across the green road.
10037{_}1112{_}When she reached it she stopped and looked with keen delight at the beautiful creatures in the fields on either side.
10037{_}1113{_}The sunshine fell upon her with loving warmth; in the distance she could hear the whirr of a mowing machine and the shouts of the men at work.
10037{_}1114{_}A magnificent young horse thrust his head familiarly over the fence near by, and under the shade of a great tree Primrose, with her graceful calf beside her, was lazily chewing her cud.
10037{_}1115{_}Everything spoke of contentment and comfort and peace.
10037{_}1116{_}An unutterable longing seized upon the lonely girl.
10037{_}1117{_}Here at least she would have God's creatures to love, and his woods and the sky!
10037{_}1118{_}She laid her head down upon the gate with a smothered cry.
10037{_}1119{_}"If I only belonged,--like the cows!"
10037{_}1120{_}"Pitty lady!"
10037{_}1121{_}Startled by the sweet, baby voice, Evadne looked up to find a pair of laughing blue eyes peeping sympathetically at her.
10037{_}1122{_}The sun-bonnet had fallen back and the golden curls were tossed in luxurious confusion over the little head.
10037{_}1123{_}Evadne caught the child in her arms.
10037{_}1124{_}"You little darling!"
10037{_}1125{_}"Yes, me is," said the child, resting contentedly within Evadne's embrace, as if, with the mysterious telepathy of childhood, she recognized a spiritual affinity which she was bound to help.
10037{_}1126{_}"Me's very nice.
10037{_}1127{_}Don says so."
10037{_}1128{_}"And who is Don?"
10037{_}1129{_}asked Evadne.
10037{_}1130{_}"Don's my bootiful man.
10037{_}1131{_}Me's doin' to marry Don when me gets big.
10037{_}1132{_}Oh, dere he is!"
10037{_}1133{_}and breaking from Evadne, she rolled herself between the bars of the gate and ran at the top of her speed towards John Randolph, who just then appeared around a bend in the road, one arm thrown lightly over the neck of the horse he had been training.
10037{_}1134{_}"Halloo, Nansie!"
10037{_}1135{_}Evadne heard his cheery greeting, saw him stoop and lift the child on to the horse's back, and was so interested in the pretty scene that she forgot she was a stranger.
10037{_}1136{_}When she came to herself with a start the little cavalcade had reached the gate and John Randolph stood before her with his hat in his hand.
10037{_}1137{_}Evadne bowed.
10037{_}1138{_}"It is so beautiful!"
10037{_}1139{_}she said.
10037{_}1140{_}"I have been waiting for my uncle and lost myself among the harmonies of Nature."
10037{_}1141{_}John Randolph's eyes lightened.
10037{_}1142{_}"It is God's world," he answered with a sweet reverence.
10037{_}1143{_}Evadne looked full into the shining face.
10037{_}1144{_}"Do you know Jesus Christ?"
10037{_}1145{_}she asked impulsively.
10037{_}1146{_}The face softened into a great tenderness.
10037{_}1147{_}"He is my King."
10037{_}1148{_}"And do you love him?"
10037{_}1149{_}"With all there is of me."
10037{_}1150{_}A servant came just then to say the Judge was waiting.
10037{_}1151{_}"I will come at once," Evadne said courteously.
10037{_}1152{_}Then she turned once more to John.
10037{_}1153{_}"And what do _you_ think of life?"
10037{_}1154{_}she cried softly.
10037{_}1155{_}"Life!"
10037{_}1156{_}he said, and there was a strange, exultant ring in his voice.
10037{_}1157{_}"Life is a beautiful possibility."
10037{_}1158{_}There was no time for more, but in the spirit realm of kinship no multitude of words is needed.
10037{_}1159{_}Only a few moments had passed, yet in that little space two souls had met.
10037{_}1160{_}What did it matter if the devious turnings of life should lead them far apart, or the barring gate of circumstance forever separate them?
10037{_}1161{_}They had found each other!
10037{_}1162{_}"Pitty lady!--Nan loves oo, dear," and the child whom John held seated on the broad top rail of the gate, held up her rosy lips for a kiss.
10037{_}1163{_}Instinctively Evadne held out her hand to John.
10037{_}1164{_}Spiritual ethics laugh at the conventionalities of time.
10037{_}1165{_}"Good-bye," she said, "and thank you."
10037{_}1166{_}She looked back once to wave her hand to little Nan.
10037{_}1167{_}John was standing as she had left him, one arm encircling the child who nestled close to him, while over his right shoulder the horse had thrust his handsome head.
10037{_}1168{_}Always afterward she saw him so.
10037{_}1169{_}It was a parable of what God had meant man to be.
10037{_}1170{_}*       *       *       *       *  Long after the sound of the carriage wheels had died away John stood motionless, beholding again as in a vision the earnest face and wonderful grey eyes.
10037{_}1171{_}Then he stooped for his hat which had fallen to the ground when he had taken her hand in his.
10037{_}1172{_}As he did so, he saw a dainty bit of lawn lying on the other side of the gate.
10037{_}1173{_}He put his hand between the bars and caught it just as the breeze was about to blow it away.
10037{_}1174{_}He looked at the name which was delicately traced in one corner with a strange sense of pleasure: Evadne.
10037{_}1175{_}"It fits her," he said to himself.
10037{_}1176{_}"There's a sweet elusiveness about her.
10037{_}1177{_}She makes me think of a bird.
10037{_}1178{_}She'll let you come just so far, until she gets to trust you, and then you'll have all her sweetness."
10037{_}1179{_}He drew a long breath which was strangely like a sigh, and, folding the handkerchief carefully, put it in his pocket.
10037{_}1180{_}"Pitty lady," murmured little Nan drowsily, and John caught her up and kissed her,--he could not have told why.
10037{_}1181{_}*       *       *       *       *  "I do think Dorothy Bruce is the kindest creature!"
10037{_}1182{_}exclaimed Marion one Saturday morning as they lingered with a pleasant sense of leisure over the breakfast table.
10037{_}1183{_}"She offered to give up the whole of to-day to me.
10037{_}1184{_}I thought it was lovely when she works so hard all the week."
10037{_}1185{_}"Give it up to you.
10037{_}1186{_}Why, what do you mean, Marion?
10037{_}1187{_}We never have anything to do with her in school.
10037{_}1188{_}What could you possibly want of her here?"
10037{_}1189{_}"Oh, it is that doleful algebra," sighed Marion.
10037{_}1190{_}"It is utterly impossible for me to get it into my head, and Dorothy takes to it like a duck to water, and she is a born teacher.
10037{_}1191{_}Madame Castle says her aptitude for imparting knowledge amounts to genius.
10037{_}1192{_}You must allow it was kind of her, Isabelle."
10037{_}1193{_}Isabelle shrugged her shoulders.
10037{_}1194{_}"Self-interested, most likely.
10037{_}1195{_}That sort of people would do anything to obtain a foothold."
10037{_}1196{_}"Oh, Isabelle!"
10037{_}1197{_}cried Evadne.
10037{_}1198{_}"Do have a little faith in your fellow-man!
10037{_}1199{_}Why should you set yourself up on a pinnacle and despise everyone who is poor, when the father of us all hoed for a living?"
10037{_}1200{_}Louis looked up from the paper he was reading.
10037{_}1201{_}"There are two things Isabelle has no faith in, Evadne.
10037{_}1202{_}The Declaration of Independence and the book she loaned you.
10037{_}1203{_}One says all men are free and equal,--the other that God has made of one blood all the nations of the earth.
10037{_}1204{_}Her Serene Highness objects to this.
10037{_}1205{_}She will have the blue blood come in somewhere, though where she gets it from heaven only knows!"
10037{_}1206{_}"Louis, I do wish you would not be so radical!"
10037{_}1207{_}Isabelle said, peevishly.
10037{_}1208{_}"You must admit there is such a thing as culture and refinement."
10037{_}1209{_}"Certainly I admit it.
10037{_}1210{_}The only thing I object to is that you talk as if you possessed a monopoly of the article, whereas I hold that it is just a question of environment.
10037{_}1211{_}It is no thanks to you that you were not born a Hottentot or a Choctaw.
10037{_}1212{_}Give yourself the same ancestors and surroundings as your chimney-sweep and wherein would you be superior to him?
10037{_}1213{_}And when it comes to ancestry, by the way, probably Miss Bruce can trace back to some of the grand old Highland chiefs who covered themselves with glory long before the lineage of Hildreth had emerged from obscurity."
10037{_}1214{_}"I don't know anyone who likes to choose his company better than you!"
10037{_}1215{_}observed Isabelle sarcastically.
10037{_}1216{_}"Certainly I do.
10037{_}1217{_}Similarity of environment presupposes similarity of tastes.
10037{_}1218{_}Probably my idea of enjoyment would not accord with the chimney-sweep's, but at the same time I don't look down on the poor beggar because he hasn't been as fortunate as I in getting his bread well buttered.
10037{_}1219{_}There is a law of cultivation for humanity as well as plants.
10037{_}1220{_}Surround a succession of generations with all the advantages of wealth, education and travel, and you produce the aristocrat; just as you get the delicate Solanum Wendlandi from the humble potato blossom.
10037{_}1221{_}Set your aristocrat in the wilderness to earn his living by the sweat of his brow,--let the rain and wind beat upon his delicate skin,--shut him away from all the elevating influences to which he has been accustomed, and, in course of time, what have you?
10037{_}1222{_}His descendants have retrograded.
10037{_}1223{_}The Solanum has become a potato again."
10037{_}1224{_}"That is all very well," said Isabelle, "but I believe the instinct of culture will be dormant somewhere."
10037{_}1225{_}"Then why do you not recognize it in your chimney-sweep?
10037{_}1226{_}For all you know he may be the descendant of some impecunious sire of a lordly house.
10037{_}1227{_}Probably plenty of them are."
10037{_}1228{_}Louis rose and tossed the paper carelessly to his mother, who had been an amused listener to the discussion.
10037{_}1229{_}It never occurred to him to do so before.
10037{_}1230{_}What did women want to know about politics or the turf?
10037{_}1231{_}"Jesus Christ never seemed to care about externals," said Evadne softly.
10037{_}1232{_}"He chose his friends among the common people."
10037{_}1233{_}"For pity's sake, Evadne!"
10037{_}1234{_}cried Isabelle.
10037{_}1235{_}"When will you learn that the Bible is not to be taken literally?"
10037{_}1236{_}"Not to be taken literally!"
10037{_}1237{_}echoed Evadne in wonderment.
10037{_}1238{_}"How is it to be taken then?"
10037{_}1239{_}"Isabelle means that we have to make allowances," said her aunt.
10037{_}1240{_}"Christ could do a great many things that you cannot."
10037{_}1241{_}Evadne was silent, while the words of Jesus kept ringing in her ears: "For I have given you an example, that ye also should do as I have done to you."
10037{_}1242{_}If only she could understand!
10037{_}1243{_}"By the way, Evadne," said Mrs. Hildreth, "I beg you will not repeat your mistake of yesterday."
10037{_}1244{_}"What do you mean, Aunt Kate?"
10037{_}1245{_}"Bringing such a disreputable character into the house.
10037{_}1246{_}When I came in and found her sitting in the hall and you talking to her I was perfectly paralyzed.
10037{_}1247{_}Horrible!
10037{_}1248{_}Why her rags were abominable, and her feet were bare!"
10037{_}1249{_}"But she had no shoes, Aunt Kate, and she was just my height.
10037{_}1250{_}I was so glad that my clothes would fit her."
10037{_}1251{_}"A pretty thing to have your clothes paraded through the streets by such a creature!
10037{_}1252{_}Most likely she would pawn them for gin.
10037{_}1253{_}I am sure she was an improper character."
10037{_}1254{_}"But, Aunt Kate," pleaded Evadne, "Jesus Christ says we must clothe the naked and feed the hungry if we would be his followers.
10037{_}1255{_}I must do as he tells me for I am going to follow him."
10037{_}1256{_}"Your uncle does enough of that for the family," said her aunt coldly.
10037{_}1257{_}"I do not wish you to try any such experiments again."
10037{_}1258{_}Puzzled and chilled, Evadne left the room.
10037{_}1259{_}Was obeying the commands of Christ only an "experiment" after all?
10037{_}1260{_}She crept up to her favorite retreat and threw herself upon her gayly covered couch.
10037{_}1261{_}"Oh, Jesus Christ!"
10037{_}1262{_}she cried passionately, "I am _glad_ I did not live in Galilee when you were there!
10037{_}1263{_}Aunt Kate and Isabelle would have thought it bad form for me to follow you in the crowd where the sinners were.
10037{_}1264{_}But they can't keep me from doing so now!
10037{_}1265{_}"Oh, I wish I were dead!
10037{_}1266{_}No one would care.
10037{_}1267{_}Yes, Pompey would be sorry.
10037{_}1268{_}Louis would call it 'a sable attachment,' but Pompey loved my father.
10037{_}1269{_}Oh, dearest!
10037{_}1270{_}dearest!"
10037{_}1271{_}She buried her head in her hands while wave after wave of desolation broke over the lonely soul.
10037{_}1272{_}"A beautiful possibility" her knight of the gate had said.
10037{_}1273{_}Could life become that to her?
10037{_}1274{_}Downstairs Pompey began to sing,--    "Shall we meet beyond the river,   Where the surges cease to roll,   Where in all the bright forever   Sorrow ne'er shall press the soul?"
10037{_}1275{_}The rich vibrations rolled up and trembled about her.
10037{_}1276{_}She held out her arms and her voice broke in a cry of triumphant faith, "Yes, we _shall_ meet, Lord Jesus, face to face!"
10037{_}1277{_}CHAPTER VIII.
10037{_}1278{_}"Pompey," said Evadne one morning, "I am going to see your wife."
10037{_}1279{_}The black face beamed with satisfaction.
10037{_}1280{_}"Dyee'll be mighty uplifted, Miss 'Vadney.
10037{_}1281{_}She think a powerful sight o' Mass Lennux."
10037{_}1282{_}Evadne stood watching him as he gave finishing touches to the silver mountings of the handsome harness.
10037{_}1283{_}"I don't believe there is another harness in Marlborough that shines like yours, Pompey," she said with a laugh.
10037{_}1284{_}"You are as particular with it as though every day was a special occasion."
10037{_}1285{_}"So 'tis, Miss 'Vadney," said Pompey simply.
10037{_}1286{_}"Can't slight nuthin' when de Lord's lookin' on.
10037{_}1287{_}Whoa, Brutis!
10037{_}1288{_}Dere's goin' ter be Holiness to de Lord written on de bells ob de horses bimeby, Missy.
10037{_}1289{_}I'se got it writ dere now."
10037{_}1290{_}"I believe you have, Pompey," said Evadne soberly, "for you do your work just as perfectly whether Uncle Lawrence is going to see it or not.
10037{_}1291{_}It almost seems as if you were trying to please someone out of sight."
10037{_}1292{_}Pompey drew himself up to his full height.
10037{_}1293{_}"I'se a frien' ob de Lord Jesus, Miss 'Vadney.
10037{_}1294{_}I'se got ter do everything perfect 'cause ob dat.
10037{_}1295{_}Couldn't bring no disgrace on my Lord."
10037{_}1296{_}"But would that disgrace him?"
10037{_}1297{_}asked Evadne in wonderment.
10037{_}1298{_}"Why, yes, Missy.
10037{_}1299{_}Ef I wuz a poor, shifles' crittur, only workin' fer de praise o' men, folks would say,--'he's no differen' frum de rest; you've got to keep yer eye on him ef yer want tings done properly.
10037{_}1300{_}De King's chillen ain't no better dan de worl's chillen be.'
10037{_}1301{_}"De Lord Jesus, he say to me,--'Pompey, you must be faithful in de little things as well as in de big.
10037{_}1302{_}I never slurred nuthin when I wuz a walkin' up and down troo Palestine.
10037{_}1303{_}I sees you, Pompey; don't make no difference whether de earthly master does or not.'
10037{_}1304{_}So I does all de little tings to de Lord, Miss 'Vadney, an' de Jedge knows he can depen' on Pompey.
10037{_}1305{_}Whenever he wants me, I'se here."
10037{_}1306{_}"That is lovely!"
10037{_}1307{_}said Evadne softly.
10037{_}1308{_}"But don't you get dreadfully tired doing the same work over and over?
10037{_}1309{_}Every day you have to do exactly the same things.
10037{_}1310{_}It is as bad as a tread-mill.
10037{_}1311{_}You just keep on going round and round."
10037{_}1312{_}Pompey gave one of his low chuckles.
10037{_}1313{_}"'Specs dat's de way in dis worl', Miss 'Vadney.
10037{_}1314{_}We'se got ter keep on eatin', an' we can't sleep enuff one night ter last fer a week,--but I 'low it's jes' one o' de beautiful laws ob de Lord,--de sun an' de moon an' de stars keeps a'goin over de same ground most continuous.
10037{_}1315{_}So long as we'se doin' his will, Missy, it don't matter much whether we'se goin' roun' an' roun' or straight ahead.
10037{_}1316{_}Stan' over, Ceesah!"
10037{_}1317{_}and Pompey gave a final polish to the horse's already immaculate legs.
10037{_}1318{_}"Why don't you blacken their hoofs, Pompey?
10037{_}1319{_}They used to do it in Barbadoes."
10037{_}1320{_}Pompey's eyes twinkled.
10037{_}1321{_}"Dat's a no 'count livery notion, Miss 'Vadney, a coverin' up de cracks an' makin' de horse's hufs look better dan dey is.
10037{_}1322{_}De King's chillens can't stoop ter any sech decepshuns.
10037{_}1323{_}De Lord Jesus says, 'Pompey, I is de truff.
10037{_}1324{_}You's got ter speak de truff an' live de truff ef you belongs ter me.'
10037{_}1325{_}We ain't got no call ter cover up anything, Miss 'Vadney, ef we'se livin' ez de Lord wants us to.
10037{_}1326{_}'Sides, der ain't no 'cashun fer it.
10037{_}1327{_}Ef we keeps de stable pure an' de food good an' gives de horse de right kind of exercise an' plenty of 'tention, de hufs will take care ob demselves," and he held Caesar's foot up for her inspection.
10037{_}1328{_}"Halloo, Evadne, are you taking lessons in farriery?
10037{_}1329{_}What's the matter, Pompey?
10037{_}1330{_}Has Caesar got a sand crack?"
10037{_}1331{_}and Louis sauntered up, the inevitable cigar between his lips.
10037{_}1332{_}"I don't 'low my horses ever hez sech things, Mass Louis," said Pompey grandly.
10037{_}1333{_}"Ha, ha!
10037{_}1334{_}what a conceited old beggar you are.
10037{_}1335{_}But I'll give the devil his due and acknowledge the horses are a credit to you."
10037{_}1336{_}He held a dollar towards him balanced on his forefinger.
10037{_}1337{_}"Here, take this and fill your pipe with it."
10037{_}1338{_}"Don't want no pay fer doin' my dooty, Mass Louis."
10037{_}1339{_}"Pshaw, man!
10037{_}1340{_}Take a tip, can't you?"
10037{_}1341{_}Pompey shook his head.
10037{_}1342{_}"I don't smoke, Mass Louis."
10037{_}1343{_}"Don't smoke!"
10037{_}1344{_}ejaculated Louis.
10037{_}1345{_}"You don't here, I know, because the Judge is afraid of fire, but you'll never make me believe that you don't spend your evenings over the fire with your pipe.
10037{_}1346{_}You darkeys are as fond of one as the other."
10037{_}1347{_}"You's mistaken, Mass Louis," said Pompey quietly.
10037{_}1348{_}"'Pon my word!
10037{_}1349{_}And why don't you smoke, Pomp?
10037{_}1350{_}You don't know what you're missing.
10037{_}1351{_}It is the greatest comfort on earth."
10037{_}1352{_}"'Specs I don't need sech poor comfort, Mass Louis.
10037{_}1353{_}I takes my comfort wid de Lord."
10037{_}1354{_}Pompey's voice was low and sweet.
10037{_}1355{_}Evadne felt her heart glow.
10037{_}1356{_}"But come now, Pomp," persisted Louis, "that's all nonsense.
10037{_}1357{_}You must have some reason for not smoking.
10037{_}1358{_}Everybody does.
10037{_}1359{_}Come, I insist on your telling me."
10037{_}1360{_}Pompey was silent for a moment.
10037{_}1361{_}"'The pure in heart shall see God,'" he said slowly.
10037{_}1362{_}"I 'low, Mass Louis, de King's chillen's got ter be pure in body too."'
10037{_}1363{_}"You insolent scoundrel!
10037{_}1364{_}How dare you?"
10037{_}1365{_}and Louis dashed the glowing end of his cigar in the negro's face.
10037{_}1366{_}For a moment Pompey stood absolutely still,--the cigar which had left its mark upon his cheek lying smouldering at his feet,--then he turned quietly and walked away.
10037{_}1367{_}Louis strode out of the coach-house.
10037{_}1368{_}Evadne followed him, her eyes blazing.
10037{_}1369{_}"You are a coward!"
10037{_}1370{_}she cried passionately.
10037{_}1371{_}"You would not have dared to do that to a man who could hit you back.
10037{_}1372{_}You forced him to tell you and then struck him for doing it!
10037{_}1373{_}If this is your culture and refinement, I despise it!
10037{_}1374{_}I am going to be a Christian, like Pompey.
10037{_}1375{_}That is grand!"
10037{_}1376{_}"Well done, coz!"
10037{_}1377{_}and Louis affected a laugh.
10037{_}1378{_}"There's not much of the 'meek and lowly' in evidence just now at any rate."
10037{_}1379{_}He looked after her as she walked away, her indignant tones still lingered in his ears.
10037{_}1380{_}"By Jove!
10037{_}1381{_}there's something to her though she is so quiet!
10037{_}1382{_}I must cultivate the child."
10037{_}1383{_}Seen through Evadne's clear eyes his action looked despicable and his better nature suggested an apology, but he swept the suggestion aside with a muttered "Pshaw!
10037{_}1384{_}he's only a nigger," and turned carelessly on his heel.
10037{_}1385{_}"You are Dyce!"
10037{_}1386{_}cried Evadne impulsively when she reached the cottage in whose open doorway a pleasant-faced colored woman was standing.
10037{_}1387{_}"Pompey has told me about you.
10037{_}1388{_}I think your husband is one of the grandest men I know."
10037{_}1389{_}"Thank you, Missy.
10037{_}1390{_}Walk right in, I'se proper glad ter see Mass Lennux's chile."
10037{_}1391{_}"Why, how did you know me?"
10037{_}1392{_}asked Evadne wonderingly.
10037{_}1393{_}The woman laughed softly.
10037{_}1394{_}"Laws, honey, you'se de livin' image of yer Pa."  She excused herself after a few moments and Evadne laid her head against the cushions of a comfortable old rocking chair and rested.
10037{_}1395{_}She wondered sometimes where her old strength had gone.
10037{_}1396{_}She had never felt tired in Barbadoes.
10037{_}1397{_}The tiny room was full of a homely comfort which did her heart good.
10037{_}1398{_}There were books lying on the table and flowers in the window, a handsome cat purred in front of the fireplace, and on a bracket in one corner an asthmatic clock ticked off the hours with wheezy vigor.
10037{_}1399{_}In an adjoining room Evadne could see a bed with its gay patchwork quilt of Dyce's making, and in the little kitchen beyond she heard her singing as she trod to and fro.
10037{_}1400{_}A couple of dainty muslin dresses were draped over chairs, for Dyce was the finest clear starcher in Marlborough, and her kitchen was all too small to hold the products of her skill.
10037{_}1401{_}She entered the room again bearing a tray covered with a snowy napkin on which were quaint blue plates of delicious bread and butter, pumpkin pie, golden browned as only Dyce could bake it, and a cup of fragrant coffee.
10037{_}1402{_}"I did not know anything could taste quite so good!"
10037{_}1403{_}Evadne said when she had finished, "you must be a wonderful cook."
10037{_}1404{_}Dyce laughed, well pleased.
10037{_}1405{_}"When de Lord gives us everything in perfecshun, 'specs it would be terrible shifles' of me ter spoil it in de cookin', Miss 'Vadney."
10037{_}1406{_}"The Lord," repeated Evadne.
10037{_}1407{_}"You know him too, then?
10037{_}1408{_}You must, if you live with Pompey."
10037{_}1409{_}Dyce's face grew luminous.
10037{_}1410{_}"He is my joy!"
10037{_}1411{_}she said softly.
10037{_}1412{_}"And does he make you happy all the time?"
10037{_}1413{_}asked the girl wistfully.
10037{_}1414{_}"You seem to have to work as hard as Pompey.
10037{_}1415{_}What is it makes you so glad?"
10037{_}1416{_}"Laws, honey, how kin I help bein' glad?
10037{_}1417{_}De chile o' de King, on de way ter my Father's palace.
10037{_}1418{_}Ain't dat enuff 'cashun ter keep a poor cullered woman rejoicin' all de day long?
10037{_}1419{_}I'se so happy I'se a singin' all de time over my work, an' in de street; it don't matter where I be."
10037{_}1420{_}"But you can't sing in the streets, Dyce!"
10037{_}1421{_}"Laws, chile, don't yer know de heart kin sing when de lips is silent?
10037{_}1422{_}It's de heart songs dat de King tinks de most of, but when de heart gits too full, den de lips hez ter do deir share."
10037{_}1423{_}"But suppose you were to lose your eyesight, or Pompey got sick, or----"  Dyce gave one of her soft laughs.
10037{_}1424{_}"Laws, honey, I never supposes.
10037{_}1425{_}De Lord's got no use fer a lot o' supposin' chillen who's allers frettin' demselves sick fer fear Satan'll git de upper han'.
10037{_}1426{_}De Lord's reignin', dat's enuff fer me.
10037{_}1427{_}I 'low he'll take care o' me in de best way."
10037{_}1428{_}Evadne looked again at the exquisitely laundered dresses.
10037{_}1429{_}"Why do you work so hard?"
10037{_}1430{_}she asked.
10037{_}1431{_}"Doesn't Pompey get enough to live on?"
10037{_}1432{_}"Oh, yes, honey; de Jedge gives good wages; but yer see, we wants to do so much fer Jesus dat de wages don't hold out."
10037{_}1433{_}"So much for Jesus!"
10037{_}1434{_}"Why, yes, Missy.
10037{_}1435{_}He says ef we loves him we'll do what he tells us, an' he's tol' us ter feed de hungry, an' clothe de naked, an' go preach de gospel.
10037{_}1436{_}So, when we cum ter talk it ober, it seem drefful shifles' in me ter be doin' nothin' when de Lord worked night an' day, so I begun ter take in laundry work an' now we hev more money ter spen' on de Lord.
10037{_}1437{_}But we never hez enuff.
10037{_}1438{_}De worl's so full o' perishin' souls an' starvin' bodies.
10037{_}1439{_}I tells Pompey I never wanted ter be rich till I began ter do de King's bizniss.
10037{_}1440{_}It's drefful comfortin' work, Miss 'Vadney."
10037{_}1441{_}*       *       *       *       *  The chill March wind blew fiercely along the streets of Marlborough one afternoon and Evadne shivered.
10037{_}1442{_}She had been standing for an hour wedged tightly against the doors of the Opera House by an impatient crowd which swayed hither and thither in a fruitless effort to force an entrance.
10037{_}1443{_}It was Signor Ferice's farewell to America and it was his whim to make his last concert a popular one, with no seats reserved.
10037{_}1444{_}Every nerve in her body seemed strained to its utmost tension and her head was in a whirl.
10037{_}1445{_}She turned and faced the crowd.
10037{_}1446{_}A sea of faces; some eager, some sullen, some frowning, all impatient.
10037{_}1447{_}The scraps of merry talk which had floated to her at intervals during the earlier stages of the waiting were no longer heard.
10037{_}1448{_}A gloomy silence seemed to have settled down upon every one.
10037{_}1449{_}Suddenly a laugh rang out upon the keen air,--so full of a clear joyousness that people involuntarily straightened their drooping shoulders, as if inspired with a new sense of vigor and smiled in sympathy.
10037{_}1450{_}Evadne started.
10037{_}1451{_}Surely she had heard that voice before!
10037{_}1452{_}It must be,--yes, it was,--her knight of the gate!
10037{_}1453{_}Their eyes met.
10037{_}1454{_}A great light swept over his face and he lifted his hat.
10037{_}1455{_}Then the surging crowd carried him out of her range of vision.
10037{_}1456{_}"I don't see what you find to look so pleased about, Evadne," grumbled Isabelle, as they drove homeward.
10037{_}1457{_}"For my part I think the whole thing was a fizzle."
10037{_}1458{_}"I was thinking," said Evadne slowly, "of the power of a laugh."
10037{_}1459{_}"The power of a laugh!
10037{_}1460{_}What in the World do you mean?"
10037{_}1461{_}"I mean that it is a great deal better for ourselves to laugh than to cry, and vastly more comfortable for our neighbors."
10037{_}1462{_}"Evadne will not be down," announced Marion the next morning as she entered the breakfast room.
10037{_}1463{_}"She caught a dreadful cold at the concert yesterday and she can't lift her head from the pillow.
10037{_}1464{_}Celestine thinks she is sickening for a fever."
10037{_}1465{_}"Dear me, how tiresome!"
10037{_}1466{_}exclaimed Mrs. Hildreth.
10037{_}1467{_}"I have such a horror of having sickness in the house,--one never knows where it will end.
10037{_}1468{_}Ring the bell for Sarah, Marion, to take up her breakfast."
10037{_}1469{_}"It is no use, Mamma.
10037{_}1470{_}She says she does not want anything."
10037{_}1471{_}"But that is nonsense.
10037{_}1472{_}The child must eat.
10037{_}1473{_}If it is fever, she will need a nurse, and nurses always make such an upheaval in a house."
10037{_}1474{_}"You had better go up, my dear, and see for yourself," said Judge Hildreth.
10037{_}1475{_}"Celestine may be mistaken."
10037{_}1476{_}"Mercy!"
10037{_}1477{_}cried Isabelle, "it is to be hoped she is!
10037{_}1478{_}I have the most abject horror of fevers and that is enough to make me catch it.
10037{_}1479{_}Fancy having one's head shorn like a convict!
10037{_}1480{_}The very idea is appalling."
10037{_}1481{_}"Oh, of course if there is the slightest danger, you and Marion will have to go to Madame Castle's to board," said her mother.
10037{_}1482{_}"It is very provoking that Evadne should have chosen to be sick just now."
10037{_}1483{_}"Not likely the poor girl had much choice in the matter," laughed Louis.
10037{_}1484{_}"There are a few things, lady mother, over which the best of us have no control."
10037{_}1485{_}"I wish you would go up and see the child, Kate," said Judge Hildreth impatiently.
10037{_}1486{_}"If there is the least fear of anything serious I will send the carriage at once for Doctor Russe.
10037{_}1487{_}It is a risky business transplanting tropical flowers into our cold climate."
10037{_}1488{_}The kind-hearted French maid was bending over Evadne's pillow when Mrs. Hildreth entered the room.
10037{_}1489{_}She had grown to love the quiet stranger whose courtesy made her work seem light, and it was with genuine regret that she whispered to her mistress,--"It is the feevar.
10037{_}1490{_}I know it well.
10037{_}1491{_}My seestar had it and died."
10037{_}1492{_}Evadne's eyes were closed and she took no notice of her aunt's entrance.
10037{_}1493{_}Mrs. Hildreth spoke to her and then left the room hurriedly to summon her husband.
10037{_}1494{_}Even her unpractised eyes showed her that her niece was very ill.
10037{_}1495{_}Doctor Russe shook his head gravely.
10037{_}1496{_}"It is a serious case," he said, "and I do not know Where you will find a nurse.
10037{_}1497{_}I never remember a spring when there was so much sickness in the city.
10037{_}1498{_}I sent my last nurse to a patient yesterday and since then have had two applications for one.
10037{_}1499{_}It is most unfortunate.
10037{_}1500{_}The young lady will need constant care.
10037{_}1501{_}She requires a person of experience."
10037{_}1502{_}Pompey, waiting to drive the doctor home, caught the words, spoken as he descended the steps to enter the carriage, and came forward eagerly.
10037{_}1503{_}"If you please, Missus," he said, touching his hat, "Dyce would come.
10037{_}1504{_}She's hed a powerful sight of 'sperience nussin' fevers in New Orleans.
10037{_}1505{_}She'd be proper glad ter tend Miss 'Vadney."
10037{_}1506{_}"How is that?"
10037{_}1507{_}questioned the busy doctor.
10037{_}1508{_}"Oh, your wife, my good fellow?
10037{_}1509{_}The very thing.
10037{_}1510{_}Let her come at once."
10037{_}1511{_}So Dyce came, and into her sympathetic ears were poured the delirious ravings of the lonely heart which had been so suddenly torn from its genial surroundings of love and happiness and thrust into the chilling atmosphere of misunderstanding and neglect.
10037{_}1512{_}Every day the patient grew weaker and after each visit the doctor looked graver.
10037{_}1513{_}Mrs. Hildreth began to feel the gnawings of remorse, as she thought of the lonely girl to whom she had so coldly refused a daughter's place; and the Judge's thoughts grew unbearable as he remembered his broken trust; even Louis missed the earnest face which he had grown to watch with a curious sense of pleasure; while the girls at school felt their hearts grow warm as they thought of the young cousin so soon to pass through the valley of the shadow.
10037{_}1514{_}But Evadne did not die.
10037{_}1515{_}The fever spent itself at last and there followed long days of utter prostration both of mind and body.
10037{_}1516{_}Dyce's cheery patience never failed.
10037{_}1517{_}Her sunny nature diffused a bright hopefulness throughout the sick chamber, until Evadne would lie in a dreamy content, almost fancying herself back in the old home as she listened to the musical tones and watched the dusky hands which so deftly ministered to her comfort.
10037{_}1518{_}One day after she had lain for a long time in silence, she looked up at her faithful nurse and the grey eyes shone like stars.
10037{_}1519{_}"Dyce!"
10037{_}1520{_}she cried softly.
10037{_}1521{_}"I have found Jesus Christ!"
10037{_}1522{_}CHAPTER IX.
10037{_}1523{_}Reginald Hawthorne lay upon a couch on the wide veranda of his lovely home.
10037{_}1524{_}The birds held high carnival around him,--nesting in the large cherry tree, playing hide and seek among the fragrant apple blossoms and making the air melodious with their merry songs.
10037{_}1525{_}Brilliant orioles flashed to and fro like gleams of gold in the sunlight, as they built their airy hammocks high among the swaying branches of the great willow, and one inquisitive robin swept boldly through the clustering vines which screened the front of the veranda and perched upon his shoulder.
10037{_}1526{_}He heard the merry hum of the bees at work and the strident call of the locusts, mingled with the distant neighing of horses and the soft lowing of the cows, but all the sweetness of nature was powerless to lift the gloom which seemed to envelop him as in a shroud.
10037{_}1527{_}His face was white and drawn with pain and there were heavy rings beneath his eyes.
10037{_}1528{_}Reginald Hawthorne would be a cripple for life.
10037{_}1529{_}The College Football Club had met a New York team in the yearly contest, which was looked forward to as one of the events in the athletic world, and Reginald had been foremost among the leaders of the play.
10037{_}1530{_}Fierce and long had been the fight and the enthusiastic spectators had shouted themselves hoarse with applause or groaned in despair when the honor of Marlborough seemed likely to be lost.
10037{_}1531{_}Then had come a mighty onward rush and the opposing forces concentrated into one seething mass of struggling humanity.
10037{_}1532{_}When they drew apart at last the College boys had made the welkin ring with shouts of victory, but their bravest champion lay white and still upon the field.
10037{_}1533{_}Long days and nights of pain had followed, when John and Mrs. Hawthorne were at their wits' end to alleviate the sufferings of the unfortunate boy.
10037{_}1534{_}Now the pain had resolved itself into a dull aching but Reginald would never walk without a crutch again.
10037{_}1535{_}The mortification to his father was extreme.
10037{_}1536{_}A passionate man, he had centred all his hopes upon his son, whose position in life he fondly expected to repay him for his years of unremitting toil, and this was the end of it all!
10037{_}1537{_}He grew daily more overbearing and hard to please, and his ebullitions of disappointment and rage were terrible to witness.
10037{_}1538{_}He vented his anger most frequently upon John, the sight of whose superb strength goaded the unhappy man into a frenzy, and John's forbearance was tried to the utmost, but there was a sweet patience growing in his soul which made it possible to endure in silence, however capricious or unreasonable the commands of his master might be, and Reginald, watching him critically, marvelled at the mysterious inner strength of his friend.
10037{_}1539{_}He came along now with his quick, light step and drew a chair up beside Reginald's couch.
10037{_}1540{_}He planned his work so as to be with the invalid as much as possible, and his constant sympathy and cheer were all that made the days bearable to him.
10037{_}1541{_}"Well, Rege, how goes it?"
10037{_}1542{_}he asked in tones as tender as a woman's.
10037{_}1543{_}Reginald looked up at him with envious eyes.
10037{_}1544{_}There was such a freshness about this strong young life, as if every moment were a separate joy.
10037{_}1545{_}"I wish I was dead!"
10037{_}1546{_}he answered moodily.
10037{_}1547{_}"Don't dare to wish that!"
10037{_}1548{_}said John quickly, "until you have made the most of your life."
10037{_}1549{_}"The most of my life!"
10037{_}1550{_}echoed Reginald contemptuously.
10037{_}1551{_}"That's well put, John, I must say!
10037{_}1552{_}What is my life worth to me now?
10037{_}1553{_}You see what my father thinks of it.
10037{_}1554{_}A useless log, as valuable as a piece of waste paper.
10037{_}1555{_}I believe it would have pleased him better if I had been killed outright.
10037{_}1556{_}He wouldn't have had the humiliation of it always before his eyes.
10037{_}1557{_}If it had been any sort of a decent accident, I believe I could bear it better, but to be knocked over in a football match, like the precious duffer that I am--bah!"
10037{_}1558{_}The concentrated bitterness of the last words made John's heart ache.
10037{_}1559{_}"Looking backward, Rege," he said quietly, "will never make a man of you.
10037{_}1560{_}It is only a waste of time and vital tissue.
10037{_}1561{_}But there are lots of noble lives in spite of limitations.
10037{_}1562{_}Paul had his thorn in the flesh, you know, and Milton his blindness.
10037{_}1563{_}Difficulties are a spur to the best that is in us."
10037{_}1564{_}"Difficulties, John.
10037{_}1565{_}You never look at them, do you?"
10037{_}1566{_}John laughed.
10037{_}1567{_}"It is not worth while except to see how to surmount them."
10037{_}1568{_}"I wish you could be idle just for an hour," said Reginald peevishly, "you make me nervous."
10037{_}1569{_}John took another stitch in the halter he was mending.
10037{_}1570{_}"Old Father Time's spoiling tooth is never still, Rege.
10037{_}1571{_}I have to work to keep pace with it."
10037{_}1572{_}"I should think you would need a month of loafing to made up for the sleep you have lost.
10037{_}1573{_}You're ahead of Napoleon, John, for he only kept one eye open, but I've never been able to catch you napping once.
10037{_}1574{_}How have you stood it, man?"
10037{_}1575{_}"Forty winks is a fair allowance sometimes, Rege."
10037{_}1576{_}Reginald groaned.
10037{_}1577{_}"Your pluck is worth a king's ransom, John.
10037{_}1578{_}I wish I had it."
10037{_}1579{_}John began to whistle softly as he drew his waxed ends in and out.
10037{_}1580{_}"I declare, John, I can't fathom you!"
10037{_}1581{_}and Reginald moved impatiently upon his couch.
10037{_}1582{_}"You are invulnerable as Achilles.
10037{_}1583{_}I never saw a fellow get so much comfort out of everything as you do, and yet your life is a steady grind.
10037{_}1584{_}What does it all mean?"
10037{_}1585{_}"It means," said John softly, "that I am a Christ's man, and he has lifted me above the power of circumstances.
10037{_}1586{_}Jesus is centre and circumference with me now, Rege.
10037{_}1587{_}"You were talking yesterday about some men wanting the earth.
10037{_}1588{_}I _own_ the earth, because it belongs to my Father,--the best part of it, you know,--there is a truer giving than by title deeds to material acres--and the world has grown very beautiful since my Father made me heir of all things through his Son.
10037{_}1589{_}The birds' songs have a new note in them, and the sunlight is brighter, and there is a different blue in the sky.
10037{_}1590{_}I'm monarch of all I survey because I get the good out of everything,--mere earthly possession doesn't amount to much, a man has to leave the finest estates behind him,--but I get the concentrated sweetness of it all wherever I am.
10037{_}1591{_}It is God's world, you know, and he is my Father."
10037{_}1592{_}John was called away just then to attend to some gentlemen who had come to look at the horses, and Reginald waited for his return in vain.
10037{_}1593{_}He heard his father's voice once, raised high in stormy wrath, then all was still again.
10037{_}1594{_}Some time afterwards, through the leafy curtain of his veranda, he saw Mr. Hawthorne drive past with a face so distorted with passion that he shivered.
10037{_}1595{_}"There's been no end of a row this time," he soliloquized.
10037{_}1596{_}"It is a mystery to me why John puts up with it.
10037{_}1597{_}He's free to go when he chooses.
10037{_}1598{_}I'm sure I'd clear out if I wasn't such a good-for-nothing.
10037{_}1599{_}The governor is getting to be more like a bear than a human being, it's a dog's life for everybody unlucky enough to be under the same roof with him."
10037{_}1600{_}*       *       *       *       *  Down at the bend of the river a tall figure lay stretched upon the moss.
10037{_}1601{_}The river laughed and the birds sang, but John Randolph's face was buried in his arms.
10037{_}1602{_}To leave Hollywood--that very night!
10037{_}1603{_}The place whose very stones were dear to him, where he had learned all he knew of home.
10037{_}1604{_}To be turned off like a beggar, without a moment's warning, after all his years of toil!
10037{_}1605{_}To say good-bye forever to the human friends who loved him, and the dear, dumb friends whom he had fondled and tended with such constant care.
10037{_}1606{_}Never again to swing along through the sweet freshness of the morning before the sun was up to find the earliest snowdrops for Mrs. Hawthorne, or take a spin in the moonlight with every nerve a-tingle across the frozen bosom of the lake, or wander in delight along the wood roads when every tree was clad in the witching beauty of a silver thaw, or sweep across the wide stretching country in the very poetry of motion, or hear the soft swish of the tall grass as it fell in fragrant rows before the mower, or the creak of the vans as they bore its ripened sweetness towards the great barns, while bird and bee and locust joined in the harmony of the Harvest Home, until the sun sank to rest amidst cloud draperies of royal purple and crimson and gold and the sweet-voiced twilight soothed the world into peace.
10037{_}1607{_}On and on the hours swept while John fought his battle.
10037{_}1608{_}At length he rose, and with long, lingering glances of good-bye to every tree and rock and flower, began his homeward way.
10037{_}1609{_}He would think of it so while he could.
10037{_}1610{_}In a few short hours he would be a wanderer upon the face of the earth.
10037{_}1611{_}A sudden joy crept into the weary eyes.
10037{_}1612{_}So was Jesus Christ!
10037{_}1613{_}"Why, John, what has happened!"
10037{_}1614{_}cried Reginald, as his faithful nurse came to make him comfortable for the night.
10037{_}1615{_}"You look like a ghost, and you have had no dinner!
10037{_}1616{_}What the mischief is to pay?
10037{_}1617{_}You must have been precious busy to leave me alone the whole afternoon."
10037{_}1618{_}"I have been, Rege," said John quietly, "very busy."
10037{_}1619{_}"I declare, John, I'd make tracks for freedom if I were in your shoes.
10037{_}1620{_}You're a regular convict, and, since you've had me on your hands, a galley slave is a gentleman of leisure in comparison!
10037{_}1621{_}Why don't you go, John?
10037{_}1622{_}You've had nothing but injustice at Hollywood."
10037{_}1623{_}John fell on his knees beside the bed.
10037{_}1624{_}"I am going, Rege.
10037{_}1625{_}Your father has ordered me away."
10037{_}1626{_}When the thought which has floated--nebulous--across our mental vision, suddenly resolves itself into tangible form and becomes a solid fact to be confronted and battled with, the shock is greater than if no shadowy premonition had ever haunted the dreamland of our fancy.
10037{_}1627{_}Reginald gave a low cry, then he lay looking at John with eyes full of a blank horror.
10037{_}1628{_}His mind utterly refused to grasp the situation.
10037{_}1629{_}"You see, Rege, it is this way," said John gently.
10037{_}1630{_}"Your father seems to have taken a dislike to me and lately I have fancied he was only waiting for an excuse to turn me off.
10037{_}1631{_}As soon as those fellows began to talk to him about the horses I saw there was trouble brewing.
10037{_}1632{_}Everything I did was wrong, and once he swore at me.
10037{_}1633{_}He would order me to bring one horse and then change his mind before I got half across the field, and then he would rail at me for not having brought the first one.
10037{_}1634{_}"They pitched on Neptune at last, and asked if he had been registered.
10037{_}1635{_}I said 'No,' so then they refused to pay the price your father asked, and he had to come down on him.
10037{_}1636{_}He was furious, and, as soon as the men's backs were turned, he ordered me out of his sight forever.
10037{_}1637{_}He says I have ruined the reputation of Hollywood," John's voice broke.
10037{_}1638{_}"But, John, you mustn't go!"
10037{_}1639{_}cried Reginald.
10037{_}1640{_}"You cannot!
10037{_}1641{_}My father is out of his mind.
10037{_}1642{_}People don't pay any attention to the ravings of a lunatic."
10037{_}1643{_}John shook his head sadly.
10037{_}1644{_}"He is master here, Rege.
10037{_}1645{_}There is nothing else for me to do."
10037{_}1646{_}"But, John, it is impossible--preposterous!
10037{_}1647{_}Why, everything will go to ruin without you, and I will take the lead."
10037{_}1648{_}"No, no!"
10037{_}1649{_}said John quickly.
10037{_}1650{_}"You will be a rich man some day, Rege.
10037{_}1651{_}Wealth is a wonderful opportunity.
10037{_}1652{_}Prepare yourself to use it well."
10037{_}1653{_}"I tell you I can't do anything without you, John.
10037{_}1654{_}I am like a ship without a rudder.
10037{_}1655{_}It is no use talking.
10037{_}1656{_}I cannot spare you.
10037{_}1657{_}You must not go!"
10037{_}1658{_}"If you take the great Pilot aboard, Rege, you will be in no danger of drifting.
10037{_}1659{_}It is only when we choose Self for our Captain that the ship runs on the rocks."
10037{_}1660{_}*       *       *       *       *  "Don, Don!"
10037{_}1661{_}The child heard his step in the hall long before he reached the door.
10037{_}1662{_}He was coming, as he did every night, to give her a ride in his arms before she went to by-by.
10037{_}1663{_}She held out her little arms from which the loose sleeves had fallen back.
10037{_}1664{_}John lifted her up, for the last time.
10037{_}1665{_}He laid his strong, set face against the rosy cheek, and looked into the laughing eyes which the sand man had already sprinkled with his magic powder.
10037{_}1666{_}"Nansie, baby, I have come to say good-bye."
10037{_}1667{_}"Not dood-bye, Don, oo always say dood-night."
10037{_}1668{_}"But it is good-bye this time, little one, there will be no more good-nights for you and me.
10037{_}1669{_}I am going away."
10037{_}1670{_}A bewildered look swept over the child's face.
10037{_}1671{_}"Away!"
10037{_}1672{_}she echoed, "to leave Nan an' Pwimwose an' the horsies?
10037{_}1673{_}Me'll do too, Don.
10037{_}1674{_}He'll do anywhere wid oo, Don."
10037{_}1675{_}"I wish I could take you!"
10037{_}1676{_}and John strained her to his breast.
10037{_}1677{_}"But there is no Neptune to carry us now, little one.
10037{_}1678{_}Your father sold him this afternoon."
10037{_}1679{_}"My nice Nepshun!"
10037{_}1680{_}The child's lip quivered, but something in the suffering face above her made her say quickly, "Me'll be dood, Don, an' when oo turn back, me'll be waitin' at de gate."
10037{_}1681{_}She patted his cheek confidingly.
10037{_}1682{_}"Nice Don!
10037{_}1683{_}Nan loves oo, dear, an' Desus.
10037{_}1684{_}Nan loves Desus 'cause oo do, Don."
10037{_}1685{_}John's voice choked.
10037{_}1686{_}"Keep on loving, Nansie."
10037{_}1687{_}"Yes, me will.
10037{_}1688{_}Does Desus carry de little chil'en in his arms like oo do, Don?
10037{_}1689{_}Me's so comf'able.
10037{_}1690{_}Me loves Desus."
10037{_}1691{_}The little arm, soft and warm, crept closer around his neck, while the golden curls swept his cheek.
10037{_}1692{_}"Oo's my bootiful man, Don.
10037{_}1693{_}Me'll marry oo when me gets big," and then, all unconscious of the sorrow which should greet her in the morning, the baby slept.
10037{_}1694{_}To and fro across the floor John trod lightly with his precious burden.
10037{_}1695{_}His arms never felt the weight.
10037{_}1696{_}They would be such empty arms bye-and-bye!
10037{_}1697{_}Then at last he laid her down, and, taking a pair of scissors from his pocket, he carefully severed one of the golden rings of hair, and laid it within the folds of the handkerchief which he still carried in his vest pocket.
10037{_}1698{_}The fair girl and the little child.
10037{_}1699{_}These should be his memory of womanhood.
10037{_}1700{_}[Illustration: 'ME'LL DO ANYWHERE, WIV OO, DON.]
10037{_}1701{_}*       *       *       *       *  In Reginald's room kind-hearted Mrs. Hawthorne was weeping bitterly.
10037{_}1702{_}She loved John as her own son, but no one ever dreamed of disputing the tyrannical dictates of the master of Hollywood, however unjust they might be.
10037{_}1703{_}Reginald lay as John had left him with his face buried in the pillows and utterly refused to be comforted.
10037{_}1704{_}What comfort could there be if John was going away?
10037{_}1705{_}It never occurred to him that his mother needed cheer as much as he.
10037{_}1706{_}Like all selfish souls his own pain completely filled his horizon.
10037{_}1707{_}CHAPTER X.
10037{_}1708{_}"I don't see what we are to do about Evadne!"
10037{_}1709{_}and Mrs. Hildreth sighed disconsolately.
10037{_}1710{_}"She looks like a walking shadow.
10037{_}1711{_}I should not be surprised if she had inherited her father's disease, and they say now that consumption is as contagious as diphtheria."
10037{_}1712{_}"Horrors!"
10037{_}1713{_}cried Isabelle.
10037{_}1714{_}"Do quarantine her somewhere, Mamma, until you are quite sure there is no danger.
10037{_}1715{_}I haven't the faintest aspirations to martyrdom."
10037{_}1716{_}"It is a great care," sighed Mrs. Hildreth.
10037{_}1717{_}"All of you children have always been so healthy.
10037{_}1718{_}I don't believe Doctor Russe will listen to her going to the seaside, and the mountains are so monotonous!
10037{_}1719{_}Other people's children are a great responsibility."
10037{_}1720{_}Suddenly Isabelle clapped her hands.
10037{_}1721{_}"I have it!"
10037{_}1722{_}she cried.
10037{_}1723{_}"Send her up to Aunt Marthe, and then we can tease Papa to let us go to Newport.
10037{_}1724{_}Marion is going to spend the summer with Christine Drayton, you know, and Papa does not intend to leave the city, so we can persuade him that it is our duty to seize such a golden opportunity of doing things economically.
10037{_}1725{_}I am sure I don't know what people must think of us, never going to any of the fashionable places.
10037{_}1726{_}For my part I think we owe it to Papa's position to keep up with the world."
10037{_}1727{_}"I believe it might be managed," said Mrs. Hildreth after some consideration.
10037{_}1728{_}"It was very clever of you to think of it, Isabelle.
10037{_}1729{_}You ought to be a diplomat, my dear," and she smiled approvingly on her daughter.
10037{_}1730{_}*       *       *       *       *  The train swept along through the picturesque Vermont scenery and Evadne looked out of her window with never ending delight.
10037{_}1731{_}"I am like a poor, lonely bird," she said to herself, "who flits from shore to shore, seeking rest and finding none.
10037{_}1732{_}Another journey in the dark!
10037{_}1733{_}I wonder what will be at the end of this one?
10037{_}1734{_}Well, I'll hope for the best.
10037{_}1735{_}Aunt Marthe's letter was kind, and her name sounds as cheery as Aunt Kate's sounds cold."
10037{_}1736{_}Mr. Everidge came to meet her as the train steamed into the little station, and Evadne soon found herself seated in a comfortable carriage behind a handsome chestnut mare, bowling along a fragrant country road, catching glimpses at every turn of the verdure-clad hills.
10037{_}1737{_}She found her new uncle very pleasant.
10037{_}1738{_}There was a silver-tongued suavity about him in striking contrast to the growing preoccupation of Judge Hildreth, and a sort of airy self complaisance which took it for granted that he should be well treated by the world.
10037{_}1739{_}"I am very glad you have come, my dear niece," he said, "to relieve the tedium of our uneventful existence.
10037{_}1740{_}You must let our Vermont air kiss the roses into bloom again in your pale cheeks.
10037{_}1741{_}It has a world-wide reputation as a tonic.
10037{_}1742{_}I hope you left our Marlborough relatives in a pleasant attitude of mind?
10037{_}1743{_}It is one of the evidences of this progressive age that you should woo 'tired Nature's sweet restorer' one night under the roof of my respected brother-in-law, the next under my own.
10037{_}1744{_}The ancients, with their primitive modes of laborious transit, were only half alive.
10037{_}1745{_}We of to-day, thanks to the melodious tea-kettle and inventive cerebral tissue of the youthful Watt, live in a perpetual hand-clasp, so to speak, and, by means of the flashing chain of light which girdles the globe are kept in touch with the world.
10037{_}1746{_}It is food for reflection that the thought which is evolved from the shadowy recesses of our brain to-day, should be, by the mysterious camera of electricity, photographed upon the retina of the Australian public to-morrow, and we need to have the archives of our memory enlarged to hold the voluminous correspondence of the century.
10037{_}1747{_}"Ah, Squire Higgins, good-evening.
10037{_}1748{_}My niece by marriage, Miss Hildreth of Barbadoes."
10037{_}1749{_}The Squire lifted his hat, there was a little desultory conversation, then the carriages went on their separate ways, and soon Evadne found herself at her destination.
10037{_}1750{_}She looked eagerly at the pretty house with its _entourage_ of flowers and lawns, grand old trees and distance-purpled hills, then Aunt Marthe appeared in the doorway and she saw nothing else.
10037{_}1751{_}She was of medium height with a crown of soft, brown hair, and eyes whose first glance of welcome caught Evadne's heart and held her captive.
10037{_}1752{_}There was a wonderful sweetness about the smiling mouth, and the face, although not classically beautiful, possessed a subtle spiritual charm more fascinating than mere physical perfection of color and form.
10037{_}1753{_}She moved lightly with a buoyant youthfulness strangely at variance with the stately dignity of Mrs. Hildreth and the studied repose of Isabelle.
10037{_}1754{_}"You dear child!"
10037{_}1755{_}The soft arms held her close, the sweet lips caught hers in a kiss, and Evadne felt with a great throb of joy that the weary bird had found a resting-place at last.
10037{_}1756{_}She led her into a cool, tastefully furnished room, drew her down beside her on the couch and took off her hat and gloves, then she handed her a fan and went to make her a lemon soda.
10037{_}1757{_}Evadne looked round the room with its soft curtains swaying in the breeze, the cool matting on the floor with a rug or two, the light bookcases with their wealth of thought, the comfortable wicker rockers, the bamboo tables holding several half cut magazines, an open work-basket, a vase with a single rose, while on the low mantel a cluster of graceful lilies were reflected in the mirror.
10037{_}1758{_}"Why, this is home!"
10037{_}1759{_}she cried and she laid her head against the cushions with a delightful sense of freedom.
10037{_}1760{_}The early supper was soon announced and Evadne found herself in a cozy dining-room seated near a window which opened into a bewildering vista of summer beauty.
10037{_}1761{_}There were flowers beside each plate as well as in the quaintly carved bowl in the centre of the table.
10037{_}1762{_}Evadne caught herself smiling.
10037{_}1763{_}That had always been a conceit of hers in Barbadoes.
10037{_}1764{_}Everything was simple but delicious.
10037{_}1765{_}The tender, juicy chicken, the delicate pink ham, the muffins browned to a turn, the Jersey butter moulded into a sheaf of wheat, and moist brown bread of Aunt Marthe's own making, the blocks of golden sponge cake, the crisp lettuce, the fragrant strawberries, the cool jelly frosted with snow.
10037{_}1766{_}Evadne drank her tea out of a chocolate tinted cup, fluted like the bell of a flower, and felt as if she were feasting on the nectar of the gods, while Mr. Everidge's silvery tones kept up a constant stream of talk and Aunt Marthe's beautiful hospitality made her feel perfectly at home.
10037{_}1767{_}"Tea, my dear Evadne," he said, as he passed her cup to be refilled, "is an infusion of poison which is slowly but surely destroying the coatings of the gastronomical organ of the female portion of society.
10037{_}1768{_}I tremble to think of the amount of tannin which analysis would show deposited in the systems of the votaries of the deadly Five o'clock, and the unhealthy nervous tension of the age is largely traceable to the excessive consumption of the pernicious liquid.
10037{_}1769{_}Chocolate, on the contrary, taken as I always drink it, is simple and nutritive, with no unpleasant after effects to be apprehended, but this decoction of bitter herbs, steeped to death in water far past its proper temperature, is concentrated lye, my dear Evadne, nothing but concentrated lye.
10037{_}1770{_}By the way, Marthe, I wish you would give your personal supervision to the preparation of my hot water in the future.
10037{_}1771{_}Nothing comparable to hot water, Evadne, just before retiring.
10037{_}1772{_}It aids digestion and induces sleep, and sleep you know is a gift of the gods.
10037{_}1773{_}The Chinese mode of punishing criminals has always seemed to me exquisite in its barbarity.
10037{_}1774{_}They simply make it impossible for the unhappy wretches to obtain a wink of sleep, until at length the torture grows unbearable and they find refuge in the long sleep which no mortal has power to prevent.
10037{_}1775{_}So, my dear Marthe, see to it if you please in future that my slumber tonic is served just on the boil.
10037{_}1776{_}The worthy Joanna does not understand the mysteries of the boiling process.
10037{_}1777{_}Water, after it has passed the initiatory stage becomes flat, absolutely flat and tasteless.
10037{_}1778{_}What I had to drink last night was so repugnant to my palate that I found it impossible to sink into repose with that calm attitude of mind which is so essential to perfect slumber.
10037{_}1779{_}"See to it also, my dear, that I am not disturbed at such an unearthly hour again as I was this morning.
10037{_}1780{_}Tesla, the great electrician, has put himself on record as intimating that the want of sleep is a potent factor in the deplorably heavy death rate of the present day.
10037{_}1781{_}He thinks sleep and longevity are synonymous, therefore it becomes us to bend every effort to attain that desirable consummation."
10037{_}1782{_}Involuntarily Evadne looked at Mrs. Everidge.
10037{_}1783{_}Her face was slightly turned towards the open window and there was a half smile upon her lips, as if, like Joan of Arc, she was listening to voices of sweeter tone than those of earth.
10037{_}1784{_}She came back to the present again on the instant and met her niece's eyes with a smile, but in the subtle realm of intuition we learn by lightning flashes, and Evadne needed no further telling to know that the saddest loneliness which can fall to the lot of a woman was the fate of her aunt.
10037{_}1785{_}Immediately after supper Mrs. Everidge persuaded Evadne to go to her room.
10037{_}1786{_}The long journey had been a great strain upon her strength and she was very tired.
10037{_}1787{_}"I wish you a good night, Uncle Horace," she said as she passed him in the doorway.
10037{_}1788{_}"And you a pleasant one," he rejoined with a gallant bow.
10037{_}1789{_}"'We are such stuff as dreams are made of--and our little life is rounded with a sleep.'"
10037{_}1790{_}She lay for a long time wakeful, revelling in the strange sense of peace which seemed to enfold her, while the evening breeze blew through the room and the twilight threw weird shadows among the dainty draperies.
10037{_}1791{_}At length there came a low knock and Mrs. Everidge opened the door.
10037{_}1792{_}Evadne stretched out her hands impulsively.
10037{_}1793{_}"Oh, this beautiful stillness!"
10037{_}1794{_}she exclaimed.
10037{_}1795{_}"In Marlborough there is the clang of the car gongs and the rumble of cabs and the tramp of feet upon the pavement until it seems as if the weary world were never to be at rest, but this house is so quiet I could almost hear a pin drop."
10037{_}1796{_}Mrs. Everidge smiled.
10037{_}1797{_}"You have quick ears, little one.
10037{_}1798{_}But we are quieter than usual to-night; Joanna is sitting up with a sick neighbor, your uncle went to his room early, and I have been reading in mine."
10037{_}1799{_}She drew a low chair up beside the bed.
10037{_}1800{_}"Now we must begin to get acquainted," she said.
10037{_}1801{_}"Dear Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}1802{_}cried Evadne, "I feel as if I had known you all my life."
10037{_}1803{_}She gave her a swift caress.
10037{_}1804{_}"You dear child!
10037{_}1805{_}Then tell me about your father."
10037{_}1806{_}Evadne looked at her gratefully.
10037{_}1807{_}No one had ever cared to know about her father before.
10037{_}1808{_}Forgetting her weariness in the absorbing interest of her subject, she talked on and on, and Mrs. Everidge with the wisdom of true sympathy, made no attempt to check her, knowing full well that the relief of the tried heart was helping her more than any physical rest could do.
10037{_}1809{_}"And now, oh, Aunt Marthe, life is so desperately lonely!"
10037{_}1810{_}she said at last with a sobbing sigh.
10037{_}1811{_}Mrs. Everidge leaned over and kissed the trembling lips.
10037{_}1812{_}"I think sometimes the earthly fatherhood is taken from us, dear child, that we may learn to know the beautiful Fatherliness of God.
10037{_}1813{_}We can never find true happiness until our restless hearts are folded close in the hush of his love.
10037{_}1814{_}Human love--however lovely--does not satisfy us.
10037{_}1815{_}Nothing can,--but God!"
10037{_}1816{_}"The Fatherliness of God," repeated Evadne.
10037{_}1817{_}"That sounds lovely, but people do not think of him so.
10037{_}1818{_}God is someone very terrible and far away."
10037{_}1819{_}"'And God shall wipe away all tears from their eyes.'
10037{_}1820{_}Does that sound as if he were far away, little one?
10037{_}1821{_}'As one whom his mother comforteth, so will I comfort you.'
10037{_}1822{_}Why, God is father and mother both to us, dear child.
10037{_}1823{_}Can you think of anyone nearer than that?"
10037{_}1824{_}Evadne caught her breath in a great gladness.
10037{_}1825{_}"I believe you are his angel of consolation," she said in a hushed voice.
10037{_}1826{_}"'Even unto them will I give ... a place and a name better than of sons and daughters,'" quoted Aunt Marthe softly.
10037{_}1827{_}"That means a location and an identity.
10037{_}1828{_}Here, sometimes, it seems as if we had neither the one nor the other.
10037{_}1829{_}Christ follows out the same idea in his picture of the abiding place which is being prepared for you and me.
10037{_}1830{_}Everything on earth is so transitory, and the human heart has such a hunger for something that will last."
10037{_}1831{_}"Have you felt this too?"
10037{_}1832{_}cried Evadne.
10037{_}1833{_}"I thought I was the only one."
10037{_}1834{_}Mrs. Everidge laughed.
10037{_}1835{_}"The only one in all the world to puzzle over its problems!
10037{_}1836{_}Oh, yes, the older we grow, the more we find that the great majority have the same feelings and perplexities as ourselves, although some may not understand their thought clearly enough to put it into words."
10037{_}1837{_}"What is your favorite verse in all the Bible?"
10037{_}1838{_}asked Evadne after a pause.
10037{_}1839{_}Mrs. Everidge laughed again, and Evadne thought she had never heard a laugh at once so merry and so sweet.
10037{_}1840{_}"You send me into a rose garden, dear child, and tell me to select the choicest bloom out of its wilderness of beauty.
10037{_}1841{_}How can I when every one has a different coloring and a fragrance all its own?
