10037{_}1842{_}Two of my special favorites are in the Revelation,--'To him that overcometh, to him will I give of the hidden manna, and I will give him a white stone, and upon the stone a new name written, which no one knoweth but he that receiveth it.'
10037{_}1843{_}'And they shall see his face, and his name shall be on their foreheads.'
10037{_}1844{_}"That means a possession and a belonging.
10037{_}1845{_}It is the spiritual symbol which binds us to our heavenly lover for eternity just as the wedding ring is a pledge of fidelity for our earth time.
10037{_}1846{_}It is only as we see it so, that we get the full beauty of the religion of Jesus.
10037{_}1847{_}His church--the inner circle of his chosen 'hidden ones'--is his bride, and what can be more glorious than to be the bride of the King of kings?
10037{_}1848{_}The dear souls who only serve him with fear do not get the sweetness out of it at all.
10037{_}1849{_}How can they, when their lives are all duty?
10037{_}1850{_}'Perfect love casteth out fear' and there is no duty about it, for when we love, it is a joy to serve and give.
10037{_}1851{_}It hurts the Christ to have us content to be simply servants when he would lift us up to the higher plane of friendship, when he has put upon us the high honor of the dearest friend of all!
10037{_}1852{_}Earthly brides spend a vast deal of time and thought over their trousseau, so I think Christ's bride should walk among men with a sweet aloofness while the spiritual garments are being fashioned in which she is to dwell with him.
10037{_}1853{_}The Bible says a great deal about dressing.
10037{_}1854{_}'Let thy garments be always white'--the sunshine color, the joy color--for bye and bye we are to walk with him in white, you know.
10037{_}1855{_}Our spiritual wardrobe must be fitted and worn down here.
10037{_}1856{_}It is a terrible mistake to put off donning the wedding robes until we come to the feast.
10037{_}1857{_}And the wardrobe is very ample.
10037{_}1858{_}Christ would have his bride luxuriously appareled.
10037{_}1859{_}'Be clothed with humility.'
10037{_}1860{_}That is a fine, close-fitting suit for every day, but over it we are to wear the garment of praise and the warm, shining robe of charity.
10037{_}1861{_}Can you fancy anything more beautiful than a life clothed in such garments as these?
10037{_}1862{_}And to me the loveliest of all is charity.
10037{_}1863{_}The highest praise I ever heard given to a woman was that 'she had such a tender way of making excuses for everybody.'
10037{_}1864{_}"Very fair must be the bride in the eyes of her royal lover, clothed in the garments which he has selected,--all light and joy and tenderness, for, the King's daughter is all glorious within."
10037{_}1865{_}"Aunt Marthe," said Evadne, after a long silence, in which they had been tasting the sweetness of it, "I do not need to ask if you know Jesus Christ?"
10037{_}1866{_}The lovely face took on an added beauty.
10037{_}1867{_}"He is my life," she said.
10037{_}1868{_}CHAPTER XI.
10037{_}1869{_}Evadne was swinging in the hammock one golden summer afternoon, humming soft snatches of her old songs while she played with her aunt's pet black and tan.
10037{_}1870{_}The sweet freshness of her new existence was rapidly restoring tone to her mental system, and life no longer seemed a hopeless task.
10037{_}1871{_}The days were full of dreamy contentment.
10037{_}1872{_}She spent long mornings under the murmuring pines in the deep belt of forest which stretched for miles behind the house, or helped Mrs. Everidge keep the rooms in dainty order; drove with her along the grass-bordered roads, while ears and eyes feasted on the symphonies of Nature and the ever changing beauty of the hills; or stood beside Joanna in a trance of delight out in the fragrant dairy, whose windows opened into a wild sweetness of fluttering leaves, and whose cool stone floor made a channel for a purling brook, watching her as with dexterous hands she shaped and moulded the bubbley dough or tossed up an omelet or made one of her delicious cherry pies, conscious through it all of the sweet influence which seemed to pervade every corner of the house and grounds.
10037{_}1873{_}"I wonder what it is about you, you dear Aunt Marthe?"
10037{_}1874{_}she soliloquized, as she pulled Noisette's silky ears.
10037{_}1875{_}"When you are away I cannot bear to go into the house,--everything seems so different, so cold and dark,--but the moment you come home again it is as lovely as ever.
10037{_}1876{_}Concentrated light.
10037{_}1877{_}Yes, that name would suit you, for light is sweet and pure and stimulating and precious.
10037{_}1878{_}If all the people in the world were like you, _what_ a world it would be!"
10037{_}1879{_}She looked up as she heard footsteps approaching, and then rose to welcome her visitor.
10037{_}1880{_}A woman twenty years her senior, bright, capable, energetic, with a shrewd face and kindly eyes whose keen glance was quick to pierce the flimsy veil of humbug, and a tongue whose good-natured sarcasm had made more than one pretender feel ashamed.
10037{_}1881{_}"How do?"
10037{_}1882{_}she said briskly, as she took the chair Evadne offered.
10037{_}1883{_}"I hope you're feelin' better sence you've cum?"
10037{_}1884{_}"Much better, thank you.
10037{_}1885{_}I am very sorry my aunt is not at home."
10037{_}1886{_}"I'm sorry likewise, though it don't make as much difference as it might have done, as I'm callin' a purpose to see you."
10037{_}1887{_}"That is very good of you," said Evadne with a laugh.
10037{_}1888{_}There was a spicy flavor about this child of the mountains which she found refreshing.
10037{_}1889{_}"It's a bit awkward," continued her visitor with a twinkle in her eye, "as we'll have to do our own introducin'.
10037{_}1890{_}My name's Penelope Riggs, Penel for brevity.
10037{_}1891{_}What's yours?"
10037{_}1892{_}"Evadne Hildreth."
10037{_}1893{_}"Evadne.
10037{_}1894{_}That's uncommon and pretty.
10037{_}1895{_}I'm goin' to call you so if you're not objectionable to it.
10037{_}1896{_}Life's too short for handles."
10037{_}1897{_}Evadne laughed merrily.
10037{_}1898{_}"I'm not in the least objectionable," she said.
10037{_}1899{_}"No, that's a fact," said her visitor after a moment's kindly scrutiny.
10037{_}1900{_}"You're true and thorough.
10037{_}1901{_}I knew I was goin' to like you when I saw you in meetin'."
10037{_}1902{_}Evadne flushed with pleasure.
10037{_}1903{_}"Why, that is a beautiful character!
10037{_}1904{_}I only wish I deserved it.
10037{_}1905{_}But I fear you are very much mistaken in me, though it is very kind in you to think such nice things."
10037{_}1906{_}"Nonsense, child!
10037{_}1907{_}I don't waste my time thinkin'.
10037{_}1908{_}Let me have a good look at your face for half an hour and I'll know as much about you as you could tell me in a week.
10037{_}1909{_}Malviny Higgins has just come back from Bosting with her head full of sykick forces an' mental affinities an' the dear knows what else, but I think it's just a cultivation of our common senses--number, five.
10037{_}1910{_}You can feel a person without touching them; it's in the air all round you; and you don't need much discrimination to know whether what you will say will hurt them or be a blessin'.
10037{_}1911{_}The main thing is to put yourself in their shoes before you begin to talk."
10037{_}1912{_}"Their shoes, Miss Riggs," laughed Evadne, "why they might not fit."
10037{_}1913{_}"Penelope," corrected her visitor, "Penel for brevity.
10037{_}1914{_}Yes, they will too, that kind of shoe leather is elastic.
10037{_}1915{_}It's the old Bible doctrine, 'never do anything to others that you wouldn't like others to do to you.'
10037{_}1916{_}If people got the shoes well fitted before they let their tongues loose, there would be a deal less sorrow and heartburn in the world."
10037{_}1917{_}"'Love thy neighbor as thyself,'" said Evadne.
10037{_}1918{_}"I never thought of it in that way before."
10037{_}1919{_}"Well," said Miss Riggs briskly, "I'm dredful glad you've cum, Evadne.
10037{_}1920{_}It'll do Mis' Everidge a sight of good to have you, though Marthe Everidge is raised above the need of humans as far as any mortal can be on this earth.
10037{_}1921{_}With all their inventions there ain't nobody discovered how to make spiritual photographs yet, or I would have the picture of _her_ character in all the windows of the land.
10037{_}1922{_}'Twould do more good than miles of tracts.
10037{_}1923{_}I agree with Paul that livin' epistles make the best readin' an' it don't seem fittin' that she should be shut up in this little place where only a few of us have the right kind of spectacles to see her through.
10037{_}1924{_}Most of the folks just allow it's Mis' Everidge's way, and would as soon think of tryin' to imitate her as a tadpole would a star."
10037{_}1925{_}"But we are to imitate Christ," said Evadne.
10037{_}1926{_}"'Course, child!
10037{_}1927{_}But it's dredful comfortin' to have a human life in front of us to show us that is possible.
10037{_}1928{_}Lots of times when life looks like a long seam an' the sewin' pricks my fingers, a new light falls on this picture, and I sez to myself, 'Penel,' says I, 'look at Marthe Everidge.
10037{_}1929{_}The Lord has made you both out of the same material.
10037{_}1930{_}There ain't no reason why she should be always gettin' nearer heaven and you goin' back to earth.
10037{_}1931{_}She has difficulties and worriments, same as you have, but if she can make every trial into a new rung for the ladder on which she is mountin' up to God, there ain't no reason why you should make a gravestone out of yours to bury yourself under; and so I start on with a new courage, an' when we get to the end of the journey, I'll not be the only one who'll have to thank Marthe Everidge for showin' the way."
10037{_}1932{_}Evadne's eyes shone.
10037{_}1933{_}"You make me feel," she cried, "as if I would rather live a beautiful life than do the most magnificent thing in the world!"
10037{_}1934{_}"That's a safe feelin' to tie to," said Penelope with an approving smile; "for character is the only thing we've got to carry with us when we go."
10037{_}1935{_}"Well," she continued, "I must be goin'.
10037{_}1936{_}I did think I'd be forehanded in callin', but mother's been dredful wakeful lately, and when daylight comes, it don't seem as if I had the ambition of a snail.
10037{_}1937{_}She don't like to be left alone for a minit, mother don't, so it's a bit of a puzzle to keep up with society."
10037{_}1938{_}She laughed cheerily as she held out her hand.
10037{_}1939{_}"Well, I'm dredful pleased to have met you.
10037{_}1940{_}I'll be more than glad to have you come in whenever you're down our way."
10037{_}1941{_}Evadne watched her as she walked briskly along the road.
10037{_}1942{_}"She is not Aunt Marthe," she said slowly; "I suppose Louis would call it a case of the solanum and the potato blossom, but she is one of the Lord's plants all the same."
10037{_}1943{_}"Aunt Marthe, what _is_ culture?"
10037{_}1944{_}she asked suddenly, as later in the afternoon Mrs. Everidge sat beside her hammock.
10037{_}1945{_}"Is Louis right?
10037{_}1946{_}Is it just the veneer of education and travel and environment?"
10037{_}1947{_}"You can hardly call that a veneer, little one.
10037{_}1948{_}Real education goes very deep.
10037{_}1949{_}Emerson says 'nothing is so indicative of deepest culture as a tender consideration of the ignorant.'
10037{_}1950{_}I think that culture, to be perfect, must have its root in love.
10037{_}1951{_}It is impossible that anyone filled with the love of Christ should ever be discourteous or lack in thoughtfulness for the feelings of others."
10037{_}1952{_}"Why that must be what Penelope Riggs meant by her 'elastic shoe leather,'" said Evadne with a laugh, and then she repeated the conversation.
10037{_}1953{_}"Oh, she has been here!
10037{_}1954{_}I am glad.
10037{_}1955{_}It will do you good to know her.
10037{_}1956{_}She is the cheeriest soul, and the busiest.
10037{_}1957{_}She always acts upon me as a tonic, for I know just how much she has had to give up and how hard her life has been."
10037{_}1958{_}"Why, Aunt Marthe, she says when she gets to heaven she will have to thank you for showing her the way.
10037{_}1959{_}She thinks you are perfection."
10037{_}1960{_}"'Not I, but Christ,'" said Aunt Marthe with a happy smile.
10037{_}1961{_}She went into the house and returned with a book in her hand.
10037{_}1962{_}"You asked what culture really was.
10037{_}1963{_}This writer says 'Drudgery.'
10037{_}1964{_}Listen while I give you a few snatches, then you shall have the book for your own.
10037{_}1965{_}"'Culture takes leisure, elegance, wide margins of time, a pocket-book; drudgery means limitations, coarseness, crowded hours, chronic worry, old clothes, black hands, headaches.
10037{_}1966{_}Our real and our ideal are not twins.
10037{_}1967{_}Never were!
10037{_}1968{_}I want the books, but the clothes basket wants me.
10037{_}1969{_}I love nature and figures are my fate.
10037{_}1970{_}My taste is books and I farm it.
10037{_}1971{_}My taste is art and I correct exercises.
10037{_}1972{_}My taste is science and I measure tape.
10037{_}1973{_}Can it be that this drudgery, not to be escaped, gives 'culture?'
10037{_}1974{_}Yes, culture of the prime elements of life, of the very fundamentals of all fine manhood and fine womanhood, the fundamentals that underlie all fulness and without which no other culture worth the winning is even possible.
10037{_}1975{_}Power of attention, power of industry, promptitude in beginning work, method and accuracy and despatch in doing it, perseverance, courage before difficulties, cheer, self-control and self-denial, they are worth more than Latin and Greek and French and German and music and art and painting and waxflowers and travels in Europe added together.
10037{_}1976{_}These last are the decorations of a man's life, those other things are the indispensables.
10037{_}1977{_}They make one's sit-fast strength and one's active momentum,--they are the solid substance of one's self.
10037{_}1978{_}"'How do we get them?
10037{_}1979{_}High school and college can give much, but these are never on their programmes.
10037{_}1980{_}All the book processes that we go to the schools for and commonly call our 'education' give no more than opportunity to win the indispensables of education.
10037{_}1981{_}We must get them somewhat as the fields and valleys get their grace.
10037{_}1982{_}Whence is it that the lines of river and meadow and hill and lake and shore conspire to-day to make the landscape beautiful?
10037{_}1983{_}Only by long chiselings and steady pressures.
10037{_}1984{_}Only by ages of glacier crush and grind, by scour of floods, by centuries of storm and sun.
10037{_}1985{_}These rounded the hills and scooped the valley-curves and mellowed the soil for meadow-grace.
10037{_}1986{_}It was 'drudgery' all over the land.
10037{_}1987{_}Mother Nature was down on her knees doing her early scrubbing work!
10037{_}1988{_}That was yesterday, to-day--result of scrubbing work--we have the laughing landscape.
10037{_}1989{_}"'Father and mother and the ancestors before them have done much to bequeath those mental qualities to us, but that which scrubs them into us, the clinch which makes them actually ours and keeps them ours, and adds to them as the years go by,--that depends on our own plod in the rut, our drill of habit, in a word our 'drudgery.'
10037{_}1990{_}It is because we have to go and go morning after morning, through rain, through shine, through toothache, headache, heartache to the appointed spot and do the appointed work, no matter what our work may be, because of the rut, plod, grind, humdrum in the work, that we get our foundations.
10037{_}1991{_}"'Drudgery is the gray angel of success, for drudgery is the doing of one thing long after it ceases to be amusing, and it is 'this one thing I do' that gathers me together from my chaos, that concentrates me from possibilities to powers and turns powers into achievements.
10037{_}1992{_}The aim in life is what the backbone is in the body, if we have no aim we have no meaning.
10037{_}1993{_}Lose us and the earth has lost nothing, no niche is empty, no force has ceased to play, for we have no aim and therefore we are still--nobody.
10037{_}1994{_}Our bodies are known and answer in this world to such or such a name, but, as to our inner selves, with real and awful meaning our walking bodies might be labelled 'An unknown man sleeps here!'
10037{_}1995{_}"'But we can be artists also in our daily task,--artists not artisans.
10037{_}1996{_}The artist is he who strives to perfect his work, the artisan strives to get through it.
10037{_}1997{_}If I cannot realize my ideal I can at least idealize my real--How?
10037{_}1998{_}By trying to be perfect in it.
10037{_}1999{_}If I am but a raindrop in a shower, I will be at least a perfect drop.
10037{_}2000{_}If but a leaf in a whole June, I will be a perfect leaf.
10037{_}2001{_}This is the beginning of all Gospels, that the kingdom of heaven is at hand just where we are.'"
10037{_}2002{_}"Oh!"
10037{_}2003{_}cried Evadne, drawing a long breath, "that is beautiful!
10037{_}2004{_}I feel as if I had been lifted up until I touched the sky."
10037{_}2005{_}"Marthe," exclaimed Mr. Everidge reproachfully, suddenly appearing in the doorway with a sock drawn over each arm, "it is incomprehensible to me you do not remember that my physical organism and darns have absolutely no affinity."
10037{_}2006{_}Mrs. Everidge laughed brightly.
10037{_}2007{_}"If you will make holes, Horace, I must make darns," she said.
10037{_}2008{_}"Not a natural sequence at all!"
10037{_}2009{_}he retorted testily.
10037{_}2010{_}"When the wear and tear of time becomes visible in my underwear it must be relegated to Reuben."
10037{_}2011{_}"But Reuben's affinity for patches may be no stronger than your own, Uncle Horace," said Evadne mischievously.
10037{_}2012{_}Mr. Everidge waved his sock-capped hands with a gesture of disdain.
10037{_}2013{_}"The lower orders, my dear Evadne, are incapable of those delicate perceptions which constitute the mental atmosphere of those of finer mould.
10037{_}2014{_}The delft does not feel the blow which would shiver the porcelain into atoms, and Reuben's epidermis is, I imagine, of such a horny consistency that he would walk in oblivious unconcern upon these elevations of needlework which are as a ploughshare to my sensitive nerves.
10037{_}2015{_}It is the penalty one has to pay for being of finer clay than the common herd of men."
10037{_}2016{_}Evadne looked at Mrs. Everidge.
10037{_}2017{_}A deep flush of shame had dyed her cheeks and her lips were quivering.
10037{_}2018{_}"Oh, Horace," she cried, "Reuben is such a faithful boy!"
10037{_}2019{_}"My dear," said her husband airily, "I make no aspersions against his moral character, but he certainly cannot be classed among the velvet-skinned aristocracy.
10037{_}2020{_}By the way, I wish you would see in future that my undergarments are of a silken texture.
10037{_}2021{_}My flesh rebels at anything approaching to harshness," and then he went complacently back to his library to weave and fashion the graceful phrases which flowed from his facile pen.
10037{_}2022{_}"Why should he go clothed in silk and you in cotton!"
10037{_}2023{_}cried Evadne, jealous for the rights of her friend.
10037{_}2024{_}Mrs. Everidge's eyes came back from one of their long journeys, "Oh, I have learned the luxury of doing without," she said lightly.
10037{_}2025{_}Evadne threw her arms around her impulsively.
10037{_}2026{_}"But why, oh, Aunt Marthe, why should not Uncle Horace learn it too?"
10037{_}2027{_}"We do not see things through the same window," she answered with a smile and a sigh.
10037{_}2028{_}CHAPTER XII.
10037{_}2029{_}John Randolph walked slowly through the soft dawning.
10037{_}2030{_}It had been a brilliant night.
10037{_}2031{_}The late moon had risen as he was bidding good-bye to the graceful creatures he should never see again, and Hollywood had been clad in a bewitching beauty which made it all the harder to say farewell.
10037{_}2032{_}Far into the night he had lingered, visiting every corner of the dearly loved home, then at last he had turned away and walked steadily along the road which led to Marlborough.
10037{_}2033{_}The sun rose in a blaze of splendor and the birds began to twitter.
10037{_}2034{_}The gripsack which he carried grew strangely heavy, and he felt faint and weary.
10037{_}2035{_}The long strain of the day before was beginning to tell upon him, and it was many hours since he had tasted food.
10037{_}2036{_}A sudden turn of the road brought him in sight of a trig little farm, against whose red gate a man was leaning, leisurely enjoying the beauty of the morning before he began work.
10037{_}2037{_}He had a pleasant face, strong and peaceful.
10037{_}2038{_}No one had ever known Joseph Makepeace to be out of temper or in a hurry.
10037{_}2039{_}He would have said it was because he commenced every day listening to the inner voice among the silences of Nature.
10037{_}2040{_}Joseph Makepeace was a Quaker.
10037{_}2041{_}"Why, John, lad!"
10037{_}2042{_}he cried, "thou art a welcome sight on this fair morning.
10037{_}2043{_}Come in, come in.
10037{_}2044{_}Breakfast will soon be ready and thou art in sore need of it by the look of thy face."
10037{_}2045{_}He gave John's hand a mighty grasp and took his gripsack from him.
10037{_}2046{_}"Why, John, hast thou walked far with this load?
10037{_}2047{_}Where were all the horses of Hollywood?
10037{_}2048{_}Is anything wrong, John?
10037{_}2049{_}I don't like thy looks, lad."
10037{_}2050{_}John's voice trembled.
10037{_}2051{_}"I have left Hollywood" he said.
10037{_}2052{_}"Mr. Hawthorne has turned me off."
10037{_}2053{_}"Left Hollywood!
10037{_}2054{_}You don't mean it, John?
10037{_}2055{_}Well, well, folks say Robert Hawthorne has not been right in his mind since his boy got hurt.
10037{_}2056{_}I believe it now.
10037{_}2057{_}It's a comfort that the great Master will never turn us off, lad.
10037{_}2058{_}Thee'd better lie down on the lounge and rest thee a bit, John, while I go and tell mother."
10037{_}2059{_}He entered the spotless kitchen where his wife was moving blithely to and fro.
10037{_}2060{_}"Thee has another 'unawares angel' to breakfast, Ruth.
10037{_}2061{_}It's a grand thing being on the public road!"
10037{_}2062{_}Ruth Makepeace laughed merrily.
10037{_}2063{_}"An angel, Joseph?
10037{_}2064{_}I hope he's not like thy last one, who stole three of my best silver spoons!"
10037{_}2065{_}"So, so, thee didst promise to forget that, Ruth, if I replace them next time I go to Marlborough."
10037{_}2066{_}"Well, so I do, except when thee does remind me.
10037{_}2067{_}Is this a very hungry angel, Joseph?
10037{_}2068{_}Does thee think I'd better cook another chicken?"
10037{_}2069{_}"He ought to be hungry, poor lad, but I doubt if he eats much.
10037{_}2070{_}Does thee remember friend Randolph, Ruth?"
10037{_}2071{_}"Of course I do.
10037{_}2072{_}But he's been dead these ten years.
10037{_}2073{_}Thee doesn't mean he's come back to breakfast with us?"
10037{_}2074{_}Her husband put his hand on her shoulder and shook her gently.
10037{_}2075{_}Then he kissed her.
10037{_}2076{_}"Thee is fractious this morning, Ruth.
10037{_}2077{_}Friend Randolph had a son, thee dost mind, whom Robert Hawthorne took to live at Hollywood.
10037{_}2078{_}It is he whom the good Lord has sent to us to care for, Ruth.
10037{_}2079{_}He's just been turned adrift."
10037{_}2080{_}"If thee wasn't so big I would shake thee, Joseph!
10037{_}2081{_}The idea of John Randolph being in this house and thee beating round the bush with thine angels!"
10037{_}2082{_}and with all her motherhood shining in her eyes, Ruth Makepeace started for the parlor.
10037{_}2083{_}In spite of the overflowing kindness with which he was surrounded John found the meal a hard one.
10037{_}2084{_}He had been used to breakfast with little Nan upon his knee.
10037{_}2085{_}"When thee is rested we'll have a talk, lad," said his host, as they rose from the table; "but thee'd better bide with us for the summer and not fret about the future: thee dost need a holiday."
10037{_}2086{_}"Of course thee dost, John!"
10037{_}2087{_}said blithe little Mrs. Makepeace.
10037{_}2088{_}"I wish thee would bide for good."
10037{_}2089{_}Her husband laid his hand upon his shoulder.
10037{_}2090{_}"Thou knowest, lad, there is the little grave out yonder.
10037{_}2091{_}Thee should'st have his place in our hearts and home.
10037{_}2092{_}Would'st thee be content to bide, John?"
10037{_}2093{_}John Randolph looked at his friends with shining eyes.
10037{_}2094{_}"You have done me good for life!"
10037{_}2095{_}he said, "but the world calls me, I must go.
10037{_}2096{_}I mean to work my way through college, and be a physician, Mr.
10037{_}2097{_}Makepeace."
10037{_}2098{_}"So!
10037{_}2099{_}so!
10037{_}2100{_}Well, we mustn't stand in the way, Ruth.
10037{_}2101{_}Thee'll make a good one, John.
10037{_}2102{_}But how art thee going to manage it, lad?"
10037{_}2103{_}"The Steel Works in Marlborough pay good wages.
10037{_}2104{_}I mean to get a place there if I can, and study in the evenings."
10037{_}2105{_}"Why, John, lad, the Steel Works shut down yesterday afternoon."
10037{_}2106{_}For an instant the brave spirit quailed, only for an instant.
10037{_}2107{_}"Then I must find something else," he said quietly.
10037{_}2108{_}"It's a bad season, John, and the times are hard."
10037{_}2109{_}Joseph Makepeace thought for a moment.
10037{_}2110{_}"There's friend Harris up the river.
10037{_}2111{_}What dost thee think, Ruth?"
10037{_}2112{_}"Why, he wants men to pile wood," exclaimed his wife.
10037{_}2113{_}"Thee would'st not set John at that!"
10037{_}2114{_}"Lincoln split rails," said John with a smile, "why should not I pile them?
10037{_}2115{_}It's clean work, and honest, Mrs.
10037{_}2116{_}Makepeace."
10037{_}2117{_}"He has a logging camp in the winter.
10037{_}2118{_}Thee would'st have good pay then, John."
10037{_}2119{_}"But thee would'st be so lonely, John, amongst all those rough men!
10037{_}2120{_}And thee did'st say once it was dangerous, Joseph.
10037{_}2121{_}It's not fit work for John."
10037{_}2122{_}"I am not afraid of work, Mrs. Makepeace, and I can never be lonely with Jesus Christ."
10037{_}2123{_}*       *       *       *       *  In far Vermont Evadne was reading aloud from a paper she had brought from the post-office.
10037{_}2124{_}"The whole sum of Christian living is just loving."
10037{_}2125{_}"Do you believe that, Aunt Marthe?"
10037{_}2126{_}"Surely, dear child.
10037{_}2127{_}Love is the fulfilling of the law, you know.
10037{_}2128{_}When we love God with our whole heart, and our neighbor as ourselves, there is no danger of our breaking the Decalogue.
10037{_}2129{_}'He who loveth knoweth God,' and 'to know him is life eternal.'"
10037{_}2130{_}"Just love," said Evadne musingly.
10037{_}2131{_}"It seems so simple."
10037{_}2132{_}"Do you think so?"
10037{_}2133{_}said Aunt Marthe with a smile.
10037{_}2134{_}"Yet people find it the hardest thing to do, as it is surely the noblest.
10037{_}2135{_}Drummond calls it 'the greatest thing in the world' and you have Paul's definition of it in Corinthians.
10037{_}2136{_}Did you ever study that to see how perfect love would make us?
10037{_}2137{_}"'Love suffereth long,' that does away with impatience; 'and is kind,' that makes us neighborly; 'love envieth not,' that saves from covetousness; 'vaunteth not itself,' that does away with self-conceit; 'seeketh not its own,' that kills selfishness; 'is not provoked,' that shows we are forgiving; 'rejoiceth not in unrighteousness,' makes us love only what is pure; 'covereth [Footnote: Marginal rendering.]
10037{_}2138{_}all things,' that leaves no room for scandal; 'believeth all things,' that does away with doubt; 'hopeth all things,' that is the antithesis of distrust; 'endureth all things,' proves that we are strong; and then the beautiful summing up of the whole matter, 'love never faileth.'
10037{_}2139{_}If that is true of us, it can only be as we are filled with the spirit of the Christ of God, 'whose nature and whose name is love.'"
10037{_}2140{_}"You see such beautiful things in the Bible!"
10037{_}2141{_}said Evadne despairingly, "why cannot I get below the surface?"
10037{_}2142{_}"You will, dearie.
10037{_}2143{_}You forget I have been digging nuggets from this precious mine for years and you have just begun to search for them.
10037{_}2144{_}Would you like another drive, or do you feel too tired?"
10037{_}2145{_}"Not in the least.
10037{_}2146{_}What can I do for you?"
10037{_}2147{_}"I would like to send some of that currant jelly I made yesterday to old Mrs. Riggs, if you are sure you would like to take it?"
10037{_}2148{_}"As sure as sure can be, dear," said Evadne with a kiss, "Where shall I find it?"
10037{_}2149{_}"In the King's corner."
10037{_}2150{_}"'The King's corner?'"
10037{_}2151{_}echoed Evadne with a puzzled look.
10037{_}2152{_}"Oh, I forgot you did not know.
10037{_}2153{_}I always give the Lord the first fruits of my cooking, and keep them in a special place set apart for his use, then, when I go to see the sick, there is always something ready to tempt their fancy.
10037{_}2154{_}It is wonderful what a saving of time it is.
10037{_}2155{_}I rarely have to make anything on purpose,--there is always something prepared."
10037{_}2156{_}She followed her niece out to the carriage, helped her pack the jelly safely, with one of her crisp loaves of fresh brown bread, bade her a merry farewell and went back to the house again singing.
10037{_}2157{_}"Oh, Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}2158{_}cried Evadne, as she drove slowly under the trees, "shall I ever, ever learn to be like you?"
10037{_}2159{_}She found the old lady sitting by the fire wrapped up in a shawl, although the day was sultry.
10037{_}2160{_}"Good-morning," said Evadne, as she deposited her parcels on the table.
10037{_}2161{_}"I come from Mrs. Everidge.
10037{_}2162{_}She thought you would fancy some of her fresh brown bread and currant jelly."
10037{_}2163{_}"Hum!"
10037{_}2164{_}said the old lady ungraciously, "I hope it's better than the last wuz.
10037{_}2165{_}Guess Mis' Everidge ain't ez pertickler ez she used ter be."
10037{_}2166{_}"Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}2167{_}cried Evadne indignantly.
10037{_}2168{_}"Why, everything she does is perfection!"
10037{_}2169{_}"Land, child!
10037{_}2170{_}There ain't no perfecshun in this world.
10037{_}2171{_}It's all a wale, a wale o' tears.
10037{_}2172{_}We'se poor, miserable critters,--wurms o' the dust,--that's what we be."
10037{_}2173{_}"There isn't any worm about Aunt Marthe," cried Evadne with a laugh.
10037{_}2174{_}"I think you must be looking through a wrong pair of spectacles, Mrs.
10037{_}2175{_}Riggs."
10037{_}2176{_}"Land, child!
10037{_}2177{_}I ain't got but the one pair, an' they got broke this morning.
10037{_}2178{_}But it's jest my luck.
10037{_}2179{_}Everything goes agin me."
10037{_}2180{_}"But you can get them mended," said Evadne.
10037{_}2181{_}"Sakes alive!
10037{_}2182{_}There ain't much hope o' gettin' them mended, with Penel behindhand on the rent, an' the firin' an' the land knows what else.
10037{_}2183{_}I don't see why Penel ain't more forehanded.
10037{_}2184{_}I tell her ef I wuz ez young an' ez spry ez she be, I guess I'd hev things different, but, la!
10037{_}2185{_}that's Penel's way.
10037{_}2186{_}She's terrible sot in her own way, Penel is.
10037{_}2187{_}She's not willin' ter take my advice.
10037{_}2188{_}Children now-a-days allers duz know more than their mothers."
10037{_}2189{_}"Where is Penelope?"
10037{_}2190{_}asked Evadne.
10037{_}2191{_}"Oh, skykin' round.
10037{_}2192{_}She's gone over to Miss Johnsing's ter help with the quiltin'.
10037{_}2193{_}That's the way she duz, an' here I am all alone with the fire ter tend ter, an' not a livin' soul ter do a hand's turn fer me!
10037{_}2194{_}She sez she hez ter do it ter keep the pot bilin'--'pears ter me Penel's pots take a sight uv bilin'."
10037{_}2195{_}"But she has left a nice pile of wood close beside you, Mrs.
10037{_}2196{_}Riggs."
10037{_}2197{_}"La, yes," grumbled the old lady, "but it's dretful thoughtless in her ter stay away so long, when she knows the stoopin' cums so hard on my rheumatiz.
10037{_}2198{_}An' it's terrible lonesome.
10037{_}2199{_}I get that narvous some days I'm all of a shake.
10037{_}2200{_}'Tain't ez ef she kep within' call, but t'other day she went clean over ter Hancocks,--a hull mile an' a half!
10037{_}2201{_}She sez she hez ter go where folks wants things done, but that's nonsense, folks oughter want things done near at hand,--they know how lonesome I be.
10037{_}2202{_}Why, a bear might cum in an' eat me up for all Penel would know.
10037{_}2203{_}She gits so taken up a' larfin' an' singin', she ain't got no sympathy.
10037{_}2204{_}Oh, it's a wale o' tears!"
10037{_}2205{_}"But there are no bears in Vernon, Mrs. Riggs," laughed Evadne.
10037{_}2206{_}"Land, child!
10037{_}2207{_}you never know what there might be!"
10037{_}2208{_}said the old lady testily.
10037{_}2209{_}"Be you a' stayin' at Mis' Everidge's?"
10037{_}2210{_}"Yes," said Evadne, "she is my aunt."
10037{_}2211{_}"Hum!
10037{_}2212{_}I never knew she hed any nieces, 'cept them two gals uv Jedge Hildreth's down ter Marlborough."
10037{_}2213{_}"I am their cousin, Mrs. Riggs.
10037{_}2214{_}I used to live in Barbadoes."
10037{_}2215{_}"Well, I declar!
10037{_}2216{_}Why, Barbaderz is t' other side of nowhere!
10037{_}2217{_}Used ter be when I went ter school.
10037{_}2218{_}Well, well, some folks hez a lion's share uv soarin' an' here I've ben all my life jest a' pinin' my heart out ter git down ter Bosting, an' I ain't never got there!
10037{_}2219{_}But that's allers the way.
10037{_}2220{_}I never git nuthin'.
10037{_}2221{_}I'm sixty-nine years old cum Christmas an' I ain't never ben further away frum hum than twenty miles hand runnin', an' here's a chit like you done travelin' enuff ter last a lifetime."
10037{_}2222{_}"But I didn't want to travel, Mrs. Riggs," said Evadne gently.
10037{_}2223{_}"I would so much rather have stayed at home."
10037{_}2224{_}"There you go!"
10037{_}2225{_}grumbled the old lady.
10037{_}2226{_}"Folks ain't never satisfied with their mercies.
10037{_}2227{_}Allers a' flyin' in the face uv Providence.
10037{_}2228{_}I tell you we'se wurms, child; miserable, shiftless wurms, a' crawlin' down in this walley of humiliation, with our faces ter the dust."
10037{_}2229{_}"But you've got a great deal to be thankful for, Mrs. Riggs," ventured Evadne, "in having such a daughter.
10037{_}2230{_}Aunt Marthe thinks she is a splendid character."
10037{_}2231{_}"So she oughter be!"
10037{_}2232{_}retorted the old lady, "with sech a bringin' up ez she's hed.
10037{_}2233{_}But land!
10037{_}2234{_}childern's dretful disappointin' ter a pusson.
10037{_}2235{_}There ain't a selfish bone in _my_ body, but Penel's ez full uv 'em.
10037{_}2236{_}She'll let me lie awake by the hour at a time while she's a' snoozin' on the sofy beside me.
10037{_}2237{_}She don't sleep in her own bed any more because I hev ter hev her handy ter rub me when the rheumatiz gits ter jumpin'.
10037{_}2238{_}She sez she can't help bein' drowsy when she's workin' through the day, but land!
10037{_}2239{_}she'd manage ter keep awake ef she hed any sympathy!
10037{_}2240{_}She ain't got no sympathy, Penel ain't; an' she ain't a bit forehanded.
10037{_}2241{_}"But I don't 'spect nuthin' else in this world.
10037{_}2242{_}It's a wale o' tears an' we ain't got nuthin' else ter look fer but triberlation an' woe.
10037{_}2243{_}Man ez born ter trouble ez the sparks fly upward, an' a woman allers hez the lion's share."
10037{_}2244{_}Evadne burst into the sitting-room with flashing eyes.
10037{_}2245{_}"Aunt Marthe, if I were Penelope Riggs, I would shoot her mother!
10037{_}2246{_}She's just a crooked old bundle of unreasonableness and ingratitude!"
10037{_}2247{_}Mrs. Everidge laughed.
10037{_}2248{_}"No, you wouldn't dear, not if you _were_ Penelope."
10037{_}2249{_}"But, Aunt Marthe, how does she stand it?
10037{_}2250{_}Why, it would drive me crazy in a week!
10037{_}2251{_}To think of that poor soul, working like a slave all day, and then grudged the few winks of sleep she gets on a hard old sofa.
10037{_}2252{_}I declare, it makes me feel hopeless!"
10037{_}2253{_}"The day I climbed Mont Blanc," said Mrs. Everidge softly, "we had a wonderful experience.
10037{_}2254{_}Down below us a sudden storm swept the valley.
10037{_}2255{_}The rain fell in torrents, and the thunder roared, but up where we stood the sun was shining and all was still.
10037{_}2256{_}When we walk with Christ, little one, we find it possible to live above the clouds."
10037{_}2257{_}"An Alpine Christian!"
10037{_}2258{_}cried Evadne.
10037{_}2259{_}"Oh, Aunt Marthe, that is beautiful!"
10037{_}2260{_}CHAPTER XIII.
10037{_}2261{_}"The ancient Egyptians, Evadne," remarked Mr. Everidge the next day at dinner, as he selected the choicest portions of a fine roast duck for his own consumption, "during the period of their nation's highest civilization, subsisted almost exclusively upon millet, dates and other fruits and cereals; and athletic Greece rose to her greatest culture upon two meals a day, consisting principally of maize and vegetables steeped in oil.
10037{_}2262{_}Don't you think you ladies would find it of advantage to copy them in this laudable abstemiousness?
10037{_}2263{_}There is something repugnant to a refined taste in the idea of eating flesh whose constituent particles partake largely of the nature of our own."
10037{_}2264{_}"Why, certainly, Uncle Horace," said Evadne merrily.
10037{_}2265{_}"I am quite ready to become a vegetarian, if you will set me the example.
10037{_}2266{_}The feminine mind, you know, is popularly supposed to be only fitted to follow a masculine lead."
10037{_}2267{_}"Ah, I wish it were possible, my dear Evadne, but the peculiar susceptibility of my internal organism precludes all thought of my making such a radical change in the matter of diet.
10037{_}2268{_}Even now, in spite of all my care, indigestion, like a grim Argus, stares me out of countenance.
10037{_}2269{_}I wish you would bear this fact more constantly in mind, my dear Marthe.
10037{_}2270{_}This duck, for instance, has not arrived at that stage of absolute fitness which is so essential to the appreciation of a delicate stomach.
10037{_}2271{_}A duck, Evadne, is a bird which requires very careful treatment in its preparation for the table.
10037{_}2272{_}It should be suspended in the air for a certain length of time, and then, after being carefully trussed, laid upon its breast in the pan, in order that all the juices of the body may concentrate in that titbit of the epicure,--then let the knife touch its richly browned skin, and, presto, you have a dish fit for the gods!
10037{_}2273{_}The skin of this duck on the contrary presents a degree of resistance to the carver which proves that it has been placed in the oven before it had arrived at that stage of perfection."
10037{_}2274{_}"Why, Horace," laughed Mrs. Everidge, "I thought this one was just right!
10037{_}2275{_}You remember you told me the last one we had, had hung five hours too long."
10037{_}2276{_}"Exactly so.
10037{_}2277{_}My friend, Trenton, will tell you that five hours is all the length of time required to seal the fate of nations.
10037{_}2278{_}It is a pet theory of his that the finale of the material world will be rapid.
10037{_}2279{_}He bases his conclusions upon the fact of the steady decrease in the volume of the surrounding atmosphere and the almost instantaneous action of all of Nature's destructive forces, fire and flood, storm and sunstroke, lightning and hail, earthquake and cyclone.
10037{_}2280{_}Oh, _apropos_ of my erudite friend, Marthe, he has promised to spend August with us, so you will have to look to your culinary laurels, for he is accustomed to dine at Delmonico's."
10037{_}2281{_}"Professor Trenton coming here in August!"
10037{_}2282{_}cried Mrs. Everidge in dismay.
10037{_}2283{_}"Why, Horace, you never told me you had invited him!"
10037{_}2284{_}"My dear, I am telling you now."
10037{_}2285{_}"But I meant to take Evadne up to our mountain camp in August.
10037{_}2286{_}I am sure the resinous air would make her strong.
10037{_}2287{_}I had my plans all laid."
10037{_}2288{_}"'The best laid plans of mice and men gang aft agley,'" said her husband suavely.
10037{_}2289{_}"Evadne's mental strength cannot fail to be developed by intercourse with such a clever man.
10037{_}2290{_}We must not allow the culture of the body to occupy so prominent a place in our thoughts that we forget the mind, you know."
10037{_}2291{_}"A fusty old Professor!"
10037{_}2292{_}pouted Evadne.
10037{_}2293{_}"Oh, Uncle Horace, why didn't you leave him among his tomes and his theories and let us be free to enjoy?"
10037{_}2294{_}"Mere sensual gratification, Evadne," said Mr. Everidge, as he replenished his plate with some dainty pickings, "is not the true aim of life.
10037{_}2295{_}I consider it a high honor that the Professor should consent to devote a month of his valuable time to my edification, for he is getting to be quite a lion in the literary world.
10037{_}2296{_}You had better have your chamber prepared for his occupancy, Marthe.
10037{_}2297{_}As I remember him at college he had a fondness amounting almost to a craze for rooms with a western aspect."
10037{_}2298{_}Joanna came in to announce the arrival of a visitor whom Evadne had already learned to dread on account of her continual depression.
10037{_}2299{_}"Oh, Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}2300{_}she exclaimed, "must you waste this beautiful afternoon listening to her dolorosities.
10037{_}2301{_}I wanted you to go for a drive!"
10037{_}2302{_}"You go, dearie, and take Penelope Riggs.
10037{_}2303{_}It will be a treat to her and you ought to be out in the open air as much as possible."
10037{_}2304{_}Evadne went out on the veranda.
10037{_}2305{_}Through the open window she could hear the visitor's ceaseless monotone of complaint mingled with the soft notes of Mrs. Everidge's cheery sympathy.
10037{_}2306{_}"Oh, dearest," she murmured, "if you had seen this beautiful life, you would have known that there is no sham in the religion of Jesus!"
10037{_}2307{_}She waited long, in the hope that Mrs. Everidge would be able to accompany her, then she started for the Eggs cottage.
10037{_}2308{_}She found the old lady alone.
10037{_}2309{_}"Where is Penelope, Mrs.
10037{_}2310{_}Riggs?"
10037{_}2311{_}"Oh, skykin' round ez usual," was the peevish response.
10037{_}2312{_}"It's church work this time.
10037{_}2313{_}When I wuz young, folks got along 'thout sech an everlastin' sight uv meetins, but nowadays there's Convenshuns, an' Auxils an' Committees, an' the land knows what, till a body's clean distracted.
10037{_}2314{_}Fer my part I hate ter see wimmen a' wallerin' round in the mud till it takes 'em the best part uv the next day ter git their skirts clean."
10037{_}2315{_}"But there is no mud now, Mrs. Riggs," laughed Evadne.
10037{_}2316{_}"Land alive, child!
10037{_}2317{_}There will be sometime.
10037{_}2318{_}In my day folks used ter stay ter hum an' mind their childern, but now they've all took ter soarin' an' it don't matter how many ends they leave flyin' loose behind 'era."
10037{_}2319{_}"But Penelope has no children to mind, Mrs.
10037{_}2320{_}Riggs."
10037{_}2321{_}"Land alive!
10037{_}2322{_}She hez me, an' I oughter be more ter her than a duzzen childern,--but she ain't got no proper feelin's, Penel ain't.
10037{_}2323{_}When I'm a' lyin' in my coffin she'll give her eyes ter hev the chance ter rub my rheumatiz, an' run for hot bottles an' flannels an' ginger tea.
10037{_}2324{_}It's an ongrateful world but I allcrs sez there ain't no use complainin'; it's what we've got ter expec',--triberlation an' anguish an' mournin' an' woe.
10037{_}2325{_}It's good enuff fer us too.
10037{_}2326{_}Sech wurms ez we be!"
10037{_}2327{_}"Well, Evadne, how do you do, child?
10037{_}2328{_}I'm dretful glad to see you," and Penelope, breezy and keen as a March wind, came bustling into the room.
10037{_}2329{_}"Why, yes, I'm well, child, if it wasn't for bein' so tumbled about in my mind."
10037{_}2330{_}"What has tumbled you, Penelope?"
10037{_}2331{_}asked Evadne with a merry laugh.
10037{_}2332{_}"The Scribes and Pharisees," was the terse rejoinder.
10037{_}2333{_}"I've just cum from a Committee meeting of the Missionary Society an' I'm free to confess my feelin's is roused tremendous.
10037{_}2334{_}Seems to me nowadays the church is built at a different angle from the Sermon on the Mount an' things is measured by the world's yardsticks till there ain't much sense in callin' it a church at all.
10037{_}2335{_}Ef you'd seen the way Squire Higgins' girls sot down on poor little Matildy Jones this afternoon, just because her father sells fish!
10037{_}2336{_}Their father sells it too, but he's got forehanded an' can do it by the gross, an' so they toss their heads an' set a whole garden full o' flowers a' shakin' upan' down.
10037{_}2337{_}They're allers more peacocky in their minds after they git their spring bunnets.
10037{_}2338{_}The Lord said we was to consider the lilies, but I guess he meant us to leave 'em in the fields, for I notice the more folks carries on the tops of their heads the less their apt to be like 'em underneath."
10037{_}2339{_}"But what did they say to her?"
10037{_}2340{_}asked Evadne.
10037{_}2341{_}"You're young, child, or you'd know there's more ways of insultin' than with the tongue, an' poor little Matildy is jest the one to be hurt that way.
10037{_}2342{_}Some folks is like clams, the minute you touch 'em, they shut themselves up in their shells an' then they don't feel what you do to 'em any more'n the Rocky mountains, but Matildy isn't made that way.
10037{_}2343{_}She just sot there with the flushes comin' in her cheeks an' the tears shinin' in her pretty eyes till my heart ached.
10037{_}2344{_}I leaned over to her an' whispered, 'Don't fret, Matildy, they ain't wuth mindin'.
10037{_}2345{_}She gave me a little wintry smile but the tears kep a' comin' an' by an' bye she got up and went out, an' ef she don't imitate the Prophet Jeremi an' water her piller with her tears this night, then I've changed my name sence mornin'.
10037{_}2346{_}"I was so uplifted in my mind with righteous indignation that I felt called upon to let it loose, so I begun in a musin' tone, as ef I was havin' a solil."
10037{_}2347{_}"'A solil?'"
10037{_}2348{_}said Evadne in a mystified tone.
10037{_}2349{_}"Why, yes; talkin' to myself, child.
10037{_}2350{_}I did think, ef there was any place folks was free an' eqal 'twould be in the Lord's service,' sez I.
10037{_}2351{_}'The Bible teaches it's a pretty dangerous bizness to offend one uv these little ones.
10037{_}2352{_}I'm not much of a hand at quotations, but there's an unpleasant connection between it an' a millstun,' sez I.
10037{_}2353{_}"Malviny Higgins tossed her head an' giv me one uv her witherinest looks, but I'm not one uv the perishin' kind, so I kep on a' musin'.
10037{_}2354{_}"'It's wonderful what a difference there is between sellin' by the poun' an' the barrel,' sez I.
10037{_}2355{_}'It's unfortunet that there's only one way to the heavenly country, an' it's a limited express with no Pullman attached.
10037{_}2356{_}The Lord hedn't time to put on a parlor car fer the wholesale trade; seems like as if it was kind uv neglectful in him.
10037{_}2357{_}It would hev been more convenient an' private.'
10037{_}2358{_}"Malviny's cheeks got as red as beets an' the flowers on her bonnet danced a Highland Fling as she leaned over to whisper somethin' to her sister, but I hed relieved my feelin's an' could join in quite peaceful like when Mrs. Songster said we'd close the meetin' by singin' 'Blest be the tie that binds.'
10037{_}2359{_}Well, there'll be no clicks in heaven, that's one blessin'."
10037{_}2360{_}"'Clicks,' Penelope?"
10037{_}2361{_}"Why, yes, child, the folks that gets off by themselves in a corner an' thinks nobody outside the circle is fit to tie their shoe.
10037{_}2362{_}I expect to hev edifyin' conversations with Moses an' Elija, an' the first thing I mean to ask him is what kind of ravens they really were."
10037{_}2363{_}"'Ravens,'" echoed Evadne bewildered, "what _do_ you mean, Penelope?"
10037{_}2364{_}"Sakes alive, child!
10037{_}2365{_}Haven't you read your Bible?
10037{_}2366{_}and don't you know the ravens fed the old gentleman in the desert, an' that folks now say they were Arabs, because the ravens are dirty birds an' live on carrion, an' it stands to reason Elija couldn't touch that if he hed an ordinary stumach.
10037{_}2367{_}As if the Lord couldn't hev made 'em bring food from the king's table if he hed chosen to do it!
10037{_}2368{_}It's all of a piece with the way folks hev now of twistin' the Bible inside out till nobody knows what it means.
10037{_}2369{_}For my part I believe if the Lord hed meant Arabs he would hev said Arabs an' not hev deceived us by callin' 'em birds uv prey.
10037{_}2370{_}Folks is so set against allowin' anything that looks like a meracle that they'll go all the way round the barn an' creep through a snake fence if they can prove it's jest an ordinary piece of business.
10037{_}2371{_}They do say there are some things the Lord can't do, but I'm free to confess I've never found them out."
10037{_}2372{_}*       *       *       *       *  "Aunt Marthe," said Evadne, when they had settled down for their evening talk, "what does it all mean?
10037{_}2373{_}'The victory of our faith,' you know, and the 'Overcomeths' in Revelation?
10037{_}2374{_}I thought Christ got the victory for us?"
10037{_}2375{_}"So he does, dear child, and we through him.
10037{_}2376{_}I came across a lovely explanation of it some time ago which I will copy for you; it has been such an inspiration.
10037{_}2377{_}Listen,--  "'When you are forgotten or neglected or purposely set at naught and you smile inwardly, glorying in the insult or the oversight,--that is victory.
10037{_}2378{_}"'When your good is evil spoken of, when your wishes are crossed, your tastes offended, your advice disregarded, your opinions ridiculed, and you take it all in patient and loving silence,--that is victory.
10037{_}2379{_}"'When you are content with any food, any raiment, any climate, any society, any position in life, any solitude, any interruption,--that is victory.
10037{_}2380{_}"'When you can bear with any discord, any annoyance, any irregularity or unpunctuality (of which you are not the cause),--that is victory.
10037{_}2381{_}"'When you can stand face to face with folly, extravagance, spiritual insensibility, contradiction of sinners, persecution, and endure it all as Jesus endured it,--that is victory.
10037{_}2382{_}"'When you never care to refer to yourself in conversation, nor to record your works, nor to seek after commendation; when you can truly love to be unknown,--that is victory.'"
10037{_}2383{_}"Now I see!"
10037{_}2384{_}exclaimed Evadne.
10037{_}2385{_}"It means the beautiful patience with which you bear aggravating things and the gentle courtesy with which you treat all sorts of troublesome people.
10037{_}2386{_}Oh, my Princess, I envy you your altitude!"
10037{_}2387{_}CHAPTER XIV.
10037{_}2388{_}Professor Trenton had come and gone and the glory of the autumn was over the land.
10037{_}2389{_}The early supper was ended and Evadne had ensconced herself in her favorite window to catch the sun's last smile before he fell asleep.
10037{_}2390{_}In the room across the hall Mr. Everidge reclined in his luxurious arm-chair and leisurely turned the pages of the last "North American Review."
10037{_}2391{_}It was Saturday evening.
10037{_}2392{_}"Why, Horace, can this be possible?"
10037{_}2393{_}Mrs. Everidge entered the room quickly and stood before her husband.
10037{_}2394{_}Neither of them noticed Evadne.
10037{_}2395{_}"My dear, many things are possible in this terrestrial sphere.
10037{_}2396{_}What particular possibility do you refer to?"
10037{_}2397{_}"That you have discharged Reuben?"
10037{_}2398{_}The sweet voice trembled.
10037{_}2399{_}Mr. Everidge's tones kept their usual complacent calm.
10037{_}2400{_}"That possibility, my dear, has taken definite form in fact."
10037{_}2401{_}"But, Horace, the boy is heart-broken."
10037{_}2402{_}"Time is a mighty healer, my love.
10037{_}2403{_}He will recover his mental equipoise in due course."
10037{_}2404{_}"But you might have given him a month's warning.
10037{_}2405{_}Where is the poor boy to find another place?
10037{_}2406{_}It is cruel to turn him off like this!"
10037{_}2407{_}"Really, my dear Marthe, I do not feel myself competent to solve all the problems of the labor question," said Mr. Everidge carelessly.
10037{_}2408{_}"Reuben must take his chances in common with the rest of his class."
10037{_}2409{_}"But, Horace, I cannot imagine what your reason for this can be!
10037{_}2410{_}Where will you find so good a boy?"
10037{_}2411{_}"I am not aware that Socrates thought it necessary to acquaint the worthy Xantippe with the reasons for his conduct," remarked Mr. Everidge suavely.
10037{_}2412{_}"The feminine mind is too much disposed to jump to hasty conclusions to prove of any assistance in deciding matters of importance.
10037{_}2413{_}The masculine brain, on the contrary, takes time for calm deliberation and weighs the pros and cons in the scale of a well balanced judgment before arriving at any definite decision.
10037{_}2414{_}But my reason in this case will soon become apparent to you.
10037{_}2415{_}I do not intend to keep a boy at all."
10037{_}2416{_}"But who will take care of Atalanta?
10037{_}2417{_}Are you going to forsake your cherished books for a curry-comb?"
10037{_}2418{_}"Really, Marthe!"
10037{_}2419{_}exclaimed her husband in an aggrieved tone, "it is incomprehensible that you should have such a total disregard for the delicacy of my constitution,--especially when you know that the very odor of the stable is abhorrent to my olfactory senses.
10037{_}2420{_}Atalanta has quarters provided for her at the Vernon Livery, and one of the grooms has orders to bring the carriage to the door at two o'clock every afternoon."
10037{_}2421{_}"But that will make it very awkward, Horace.
10037{_}2422{_}I so often have to use the carriage in the morning."
10037{_}2423{_}"'Have,' my dear Marthe, is a word which admits of many substitutions,--'cannot' in this case will be a suitable one.
10037{_}2424{_}I find it is necessary to resume possession of the reins.
10037{_}2425{_}Atalanta is retrograding and is rapidly losing that characteristic of speed which made her name a fitting one.
10037{_}2426{_}There is a lack of mastery about a woman's handling of the ribbons which is quickly detected by horses, especially when they are of more than average intelligence."
10037{_}2427{_}"But, Horace, if Reuben goes, Joanna will go too.
10037{_}2428{_}You know she promised her mother she would never leave him."
10037{_}2429{_}"In that event, my dear, you will have an opportunity to become more intimately acquainted with the mysteries of the culinary art," observed Mr. Everidge cheerfully.
10037{_}2430{_}"It will be a splendid chance to evolve that finest of character combinations, Spartan endurance coupled with American progressiveness."
10037{_}2431{_}Mrs. Everidge smiled.
10037{_}2432{_}"But what if I do not have the Spartan strength, Horace?"
10037{_}2433{_}"That is merely a matter of imagination, my love.
10037{_}2434{_}It proves the truth of my theory that necessity develops capacity.
10037{_}2435{_}A woman of leisure, for want of suitable mental pabulum, grows to fancy she has every ill that flesh is heir to, whereas, when she is obliged by compelling circumstances to put her muscles into practice, her mind acquires a more healthy tone.
10037{_}2436{_}Self-contemplation is a most enervating exercise and involves a tremendous drain on the moral forces."
10037{_}2437{_}"Do you think I waste much time in that way, Horace?"
10037{_}2438{_}Mrs. Everidge spoke wistfully, and Evadne, forced to be an unwilling listener to the conversation, felt her cheeks grow hot with indignation.
10037{_}2439{_}"My dear, I merely refer to the deplorable tendency of your sex.
10037{_}2440{_}All you require is moral stamina to tear yourself away from the arms of Morpheus at an earlier hour in the It is a popular illusion, you know, that work performed before sunrise takes less time to accomplish and is better done than later in the day.
10037{_}2441{_}My mother used to affirm that she accomplished the work of two days in one when she arose at three a.m., but then my mother was a most exceptional woman," with which parting thrust Mr. Everidge retired behind the pages of his magazine.
10037{_}2442{_}Upstairs in her own room Evadne paced the floor with tightly clenched hands.
10037{_}2443{_}"Oh!"
10037{_}2444{_}she cried, "what shall I do?
10037{_}2445{_}I hate him!
10037{_}2446{_}I hate him!
10037{_}2447{_}How dare he!
10037{_}2448{_}He ought to be glad to go down on his knees to serve her, she is so sweet, so dear!
10037{_}2449{_}Oh, I cannot bear it!
10037{_}2450{_}That she should be compelled to endure such servitude, and I can do nothing to help, nothing!
10037{_}2451{_}nothing!"
10037{_}2452{_}She threw herself across the bed and burst into a passion of tears.
10037{_}2453{_}Was this the silent girl whom Isabelle had voted tiresome and slow?
10037{_}2454{_}A little later than usual she heard the low knock which always preceded the visit which she looked forward to as the sweetest part of the day.
10037{_}2455{_}Could it be possible she would come to-night?
10037{_}2456{_}Was no thought of self ever permitted to enter that brave, suffering heart?
10037{_}2457{_}She rose and opened the door.
10037{_}2458{_}The dear face was paler than usual but there was no shadow upon the smooth brow.
10037{_}2459{_}Marthe Everidge had crossed the tempest-tossed ocean of human passion into the sun-kissed calm of Christ's perfect peace.
10037{_}2460{_}Evadne threw her arms around her neck and laid her storm-swept face upon her shoulder.
10037{_}2461{_}"Forgive me!"
10037{_}2462{_}she cried, "I heard it all.
10037{_}2463{_}I could not help it.
10037{_}2464{_}I think my heart is breaking.
10037{_}2465{_}Do not be angry, you see I love you so!
10037{_}2466{_}How can I bear to have you subjected to this?
10037{_}2467{_}You are so tender, so true.
10037{_}2468{_}There is such a charm about you!
10037{_}2469{_}You are so beautifully unselfish!
10037{_}2470{_}Oh, my dear, my dear, how can you, do you bear it?"
10037{_}2471{_}Mrs. Everidge lifted her face tenderly and kissed the quivering lips.
10037{_}2472{_}"It is 'not I but Christ,' dear child.
10037{_}2473{_}That makes it possible."
10037{_}2474{_}Then she drew her over to the lounge and began to undress her as if she had been a baby.
10037{_}2475{_}"My dear little sister.
10037{_}2476{_}You are utterly exhausted.
10037{_}2477{_}You are not strong enough to suffer so."
10037{_}2478{_}"Oh, will you let me be your sister and help you bear your burdens?"
10037{_}2479{_}cried Evadne, unconscious that all the time the skilful hands were keeping up their sweet ministry and that her burden was being lifted for her by the one who had the greater burden to bear.
10037{_}2480{_}When she was comfortably settled for the night Mrs. Everidge drew her low chair up beside the bed.
10037{_}2481{_}Evadne caught her hand in hers and kissed it reverently.
10037{_}2482{_}"I wish I could make you understand how I honor you!"
10037{_}2483{_}she said.
10037{_}2484{_}"You must not do it, dear!"
10037{_}2485{_}said Aunt Marthe quickly.
10037{_}2486{_}"Honor the King."
10037{_}2487{_}After a pause she began to speak slowly and her voice was sweet and low.
10037{_}2488{_}"When, the first night you came, you asked me if I knew Jesus Christ, I told you he was my life.
10037{_}2489{_}That explains it all.
10037{_}2490{_}It is very sweet of you to say the kind things that you have about me but they are not true.
10037{_}2491{_}In and of herself, Marthe Everidge is nothing.
10037{_}2492{_}The moment she tries to live her own life she utterly fails.
10037{_}2493{_}If there is anything good about her life, it is only as she lets Christ live it for her."
10037{_}2494{_}"I do not understand," said Evadne with a puzzled look.
10037{_}2495{_}"How is it possible for any one else to live our lives for us?"
10037{_}2496{_}"No one can but Jesus," said Aunt Marthe with a smile.
10037{_}2497{_}"He does the impossible.
10037{_}2498{_}Take that exquisite fifteenth chapter of St. John and study it verse by verse.
10037{_}2499{_}'Abide in me, and I in you.'
10037{_}2500{_}There you have the two abidings.
10037{_}2501{_}We are _in_ Christ when we believe in him and are accepted through the merit of his blood and brought by adoption into the family of God, but not until he abides in our hearts shall our lives become as beautiful as God means them to be.
10037{_}2502{_}Fruitfulness,--that is the cry everywhere.
10037{_}2503{_}Men are calling for intellectual fruitfulness and mechanical fruitfulness, and are bending their energies to find the soil which will develop at once the best quality and greatest amount of fruit.
10037{_}2504{_}Take a tree, to make my meaning clearer.
10037{_}2505{_}The tree may abide in the soil and be just alive, but it is not until the essence of the soil enters into and abides in the tree, that it really grows and bears fruit.
10037{_}2506{_}Growers of the finest varieties will show you plums that look as if they had been frosted with silver, and peaches with cheeks like the first blush of dawn.
10037{_}2507{_}The 'fruits of the Spirit,' have a wondrous bloom and an exquisite fragrance."
10037{_}2508{_}"'Love, joy, peace,'" Evadne repeated slowly, "'long-suffering, gentleness, goodness, faith.'
10037{_}2509{_}But those belong to the Spirit, Aunt Marthe."
10037{_}2510{_}"Yes, dear child, the Spirit of Jesus.
10037{_}2511{_}The Spirit whom he sent to comfort his people when he took his bodily presence from the earth.
10037{_}2512{_}The holy, indwelling presence which is to reveal the Christ to us and prepare us for the abiding of the Father and the Son.
10037{_}2513{_}It is the beautiful mystery of the Trinity."
10037{_}2514{_}"But we cannot have the Trinity abiding in our hearts!"
10037{_}2515{_}said Evadne in an awestruck voice.
10037{_}2516{_}"The Bible teaches us so."
10037{_}2517{_}"Not God, Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}2518{_}"Jesus is God, little one.
10037{_}2519{_}He said to the Jews, 'I and my Father are one.'
10037{_}2520{_}He says plainly, 'If any man love me, he will keep my word and my Father will love him, and we will come unto him and make our abode with him,' and in another place we are told to be filled with the Spirit.
10037{_}2521{_}It is three persons but three in one."
10037{_}2522{_}"I do not understand, Aunt Marthe."
10037{_}2523{_}"No, dear, we never shall, down here.
10037{_}2524{_}Thomas wanted to do that and Christ said 'Blessed are they that have not seen and yet have believed.'
10037{_}2525{_}The Spirit is continually giving us deeper insight into the love of the Son, just as the Son came to make known to the world the wonderful love of the Father."
10037{_}2526{_}"But 'be filled,'" said Evadne.
10037{_}2527{_}"That looks as if we had something to do with it."
10037{_}2528{_}"So we have, dear child.
10037{_}2529{_}Suppose a man owned one hundred acres of land and gave you the right of way through it from one public road to another,--that would leave him many acres for his own use on which you have no right to trespass.
10037{_}2530{_}I think we treat Jesus so.
10037{_}2531{_}We are willing that he should have the right of way through our hearts, but we forget that every acre must be the King's property.
10037{_}2532{_}There must be no rights reserved, no fenced corners.
10037{_}2533{_}Jesus must be an absolute monarch."
10037{_}2534{_}Mrs. Everidge spoke the last words softly and Evadne, looking at her uplifted face, shining now with the radiance which always filled it when she spoke of her Lord, saw again that glowing face which she had watched across the gate at Hollywood and heard the strange, exultant tones, 'He is my King!'
10037{_}2535{_}Ah, that was beautiful!
10037{_}2536{_}That was what Aunt Marthe meant, and Pompey and Dyce.
10037{_}2537{_}"Jesus must come to abide, not merely as a transient guest," Aunt Marthe continued in her low tones.
10037{_}2538{_}"We must give him full control of our thought and will.
10037{_}2539{_}We must hand him the keys of the citadel.
10037{_}2540{_}We must give the all for the all,--that is only fair dealing.
10037{_}2541{_}You see, dear child, Christ cannot fill us until we are willing to be emptied of self.
10037{_}2542{_}He must have undivided possession.
10037{_}2543{_}There is a vast amount of heartache, little one, in this old world, and self is at the bottom of it all, when we stop to analyze it.
10037{_}2544{_}We want to be first, to be thought much of, to be loved best.
10037{_}2545{_}No wonder that the selfless life seems impossible to most people.
10037{_}2546{_}Think what a continuous self-sacrifice Christ's life was!
10037{_}2547{_}So utterly alone and lonely among such uncongenial surroundings with people uncouth and totally foreign to his tastes.
10037{_}2548{_}Ah!
10037{_}2549{_}we don't realize it.
10037{_}2550{_}We look at him doing the splendid things amidst the plaudits of the multitude, but think of the monotonous, weary days, going up and down the sun-baked streets surrounded by a crowd of noisy beggars full of all sorts of loathsome disease, and the humdrum life in Nazareth; and all the time the great heart aching with that ceaseless sorrow,--'His own received him not!'
10037{_}2551{_}Oh, what a waste of love!
10037{_}2552{_}We do not realize that it is in these footsteps of his that we are called to follow.
10037{_}2553{_}We are willing to do the great things, with the world looking on, but not for the loneliness and the pain!
10037{_}2554{_}It seems a strange antithesis that Paul should count that as his highest glory, and yet how comparatively few seem counted worthy to enter with Christ into the shadow of that mysterious Gethsemane which lasted all his life.
10037{_}2555{_}'The fellowship of his sufferings.'
10037{_}2556{_}It must surely mean the privilege of getting very near his heart, just as human hearts grow closer in a common sorrow,--knit by pain.
10037{_}2557{_}Yes, dear child, self must die: and it is a cruel death,--the death of the cross.
10037{_}2558{_}But then comes the newness of life with its strange, sweet joy which the world's children do not know the taste of.
10037{_}2559{_}How can they when it is 'the joy of the Lord,' and they reject him?"
10037{_}2560{_}"You talk of the cross, Aunt Marthe, and other people talk of crosses.
10037{_}2561{_}Aunt Kate and Isabelle are always talking about the sacrifices they have to make, and Mrs. Rivers carries a perfect bundle of crosses on her back.
10037{_}2562{_}She is wealthy and has everything she wants, and yet she is always wailing, while Dyce is as happy as the day is long.
10037{_}2563{_}Do the poor Christians always do the singing while the rich ones sigh?"
10037{_}2564{_}Mrs. Everidge smiled.
10037{_}2565{_}"We make our crosses, dear child, when we put our wishes at right angles to God's will.
10037{_}2566{_}When we only care to please him everything that he chooses for us seems just right.
10037{_}2567{_}I have heard people speak as if it were a cross to mention the name of Christ.
10037{_}2568{_}How could it be if they loved him?
10037{_}2569{_}Do you find it a cross to talk to me about your father?
10037{_}2570{_}People make a terrible mistake about this.
10037{_}2571{_}The only cross we are commanded to carry is the cross of Christ."
10037{_}2572{_}"And what is that, Aunt Marthe?"
10037{_}2573{_}"Self renunciation," said Aunt Marthe softly, "the secret of peace.
10037{_}2574{_}"Among all the pictures of the Madonna," she continued after a pause, "the one I like best is where Mary is sitting, holding in her hands the crown of thorns; everything else had been wrenched from her grasp, but this they had no use for.
10037{_}2575{_}What a legacy it was!
10037{_}2576{_}As I look at it I see how he has gathered all the thorns of life and woven them into that kingly garland which is his glory.
10037{_}2577{_}All the wealth of the Indies could not shed as dazzling a light as that thorny crown.
10037{_}2578{_}Like the brave soldier who gathered into his own breast the spears of the enemy, Christ has taken the sting from our sorrows and made us more than conquerors over the wounds of earth.
10037{_}2579{_}Surely he has tasted it all for us,--the baseness and coldness and ingratitude and treachery which have wrung human hearts all through the ages,--when Judas betrayed him, Peter denied him and they all forsook him and fled, do you suppose any other pain was comparable to that?
10037{_}2580{_}Only our friends have the power to wound us, you know, and, 'he was wounded in the house of his friends.'
10037{_}2581{_}When people talk of the crucifixion they think of the nail-torn hands and pierced side,--I think of his heart!
10037{_}2582{_}Oh, my Lord, how _could_ they treat thee so!"
10037{_}2583{_}Evadne looked wistfully at the rapt face, irradiated now by the moonlight which was streaming in through the window.
10037{_}2584{_}"_How_ you love him, Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}2585{_}"He is my all," she answered simply.
10037{_}2586{_}The girl stroked the hand which she still held in both her own.
10037{_}2587{_}She is absolutely satisfied, she thought sorrowfully, she wants nothing that I can give her.
10037{_}2588{_}And then through the stillness she heard the sweet voice singing,--    "I love thee because thou hast first loved me,   And purchased my pardon on Calvary's tree;   I love thee for wearing the thorns on thy brow,   If ever I loved thee, my Jesus, 'tis now."
10037{_}2589{_}CHAPTER XV.
10037{_}2590{_}"Dear Aunt Marthe," cried Evadne one afternoon, "what is love?"
10037{_}2591{_}"I will answer you in the words of one who for years has lived the love-life," said Mrs. Everidge.
10037{_}2592{_}"'One must be himself infinite in knowledge to define it, infinite in comprehension to fathom it, infinite in love to appreciate it.
10037{_}2593{_}Love is God in man, for "God is love," and "every one that loveth is born of God;" but love is not merely veneration, nor respect, nor justice, nor passion, nor jealousy, nor sympathy, nor pity, nor self-gratification; to love something as our own is but a form of self-love; to love something in order to win it for ourselves is just a perpetration of the same mistake.'
10037{_}2594{_}Dr. Karl Gerok wrote,--'Love is the fundamental law of the world.
10037{_}2595{_}First, as written in heaven, for God is love; second, as written on the cross, for Christ is love; third, as written in our hearts, for Christianity is love,' And Drummond tells us that 'Love--is the rule for fulfilling all rules, the new commandment for keeping all the old commandments, Christ's one secret of the Christian life.'
10037{_}2596{_}And another writer says,--'You are a personality only as your heart lives, and the heart lives only as it loves.
10037{_}2597{_}Love is all action, therefore the amount of your active love measures the size of your personal heart.'"
10037{_}2598{_}"Love has been defined as 'the desire to bless.'
10037{_}2599{_}That is like divine love, for there can be no self thought in God.
10037{_}2600{_}God's love is over all and above all, but when our love responds to his, his love becomes to us a personal experience.
10037{_}2601{_}Love can reach down when in loving trust we reach up.
10037{_}2602{_}Love is like the seed.
10037{_}2603{_}It manifests no life until it begins to grow.
10037{_}2604{_}Like the seed it must rise out of the dark ground into the light of heaven,--out of self thought into God.
10037{_}2605{_}God's love to us is like the sunlight.
10037{_}2606{_}We can make it our own only by being in it, if we try to shut up the sunlight, we shut it out.
10037{_}2607{_}We forget to do wrong when loving God.
10037{_}2608{_}As we love God, the love we feel for him goes out to others."
10037{_}2609{_}Evadne sighed.
10037{_}2610{_}"You make it seem a wonderful thing to be a Christian," she said.
10037{_}2611{_}"To be a Christian, little one, Andrew Murray tells us, 'just means to have Christ's love.'
10037{_}2612{_}Real love means giving always, of our best."
10037{_}2613{_}[Illustration: THE SILENT FIGURE WITH THE AWFUL ENTREATY IN ITS STARING EYES]  God so loved that he gave his Son, the essence of himself.
10037{_}2614{_}Jesus gave his life, not only in the final agony of the crucifixion, but all through the beautiful years of ministry in Nazareth and Galilee.
10037{_}2615{_}There is a truer giving than of our temporal goods.
10037{_}2616{_}Our friends, if they really love us, want most of all what we can give them of ourselves.
10037{_}2617{_}It is those who give themselves to the world's need who come nearest to the divine pattern Christ has set for us to copy, and, if we truly love him, we shall want not his gifts but himself.
10037{_}2618{_}"People seek after holy living instead of perfect loving, they do not realize that we can be truly holy only as we love, for 'love is the great reality of the spiritual world.'"
10037{_}2619{_}Evadne laid her cheek caressingly against Mrs. Everidge's.
10037{_}2620{_}"If it were only you, dear, how delightfully easy it would be, but do you suppose it is possible for me to love Aunt Kate and Isabelle?"
10037{_}2621{_}"Yes, dear child, with the love of God."
10037{_}2622{_}"You can't imagine how I dread the idea of going back!"
10037{_}2623{_}Evadne said with a sigh.
10037{_}2624{_}"This summer has been like a lovely dream.
10037{_}2625{_}How shall I endure the cold reality of my waking?"
10037{_}2626{_}"Where is your joy, little one?"
10037{_}2627{_}"Joy, Aunt Marthe!"
10037{_}2628{_}exclaimed Evadne drearily, "why, I haven't got any apart from you.
10037{_}2629{_}Just the mere thought of the separation makes my heart ache."
10037{_}2630{_}"'The joy of the Lord,'" said Mrs. Everidge softly.
10037{_}2631{_}"If Jesus Christ is able to fill heaven don't you think he ought to be able to fill earth too?
10037{_}2632{_}The trouble is we turn away from him and pour our wealth of love at earthly shrines.
10037{_}2633{_}Mary showed us the better way,--she _broke_ the box, that every drop of the precious ointment might fall on his dear head.
10037{_}2634{_}What is going to be the crowning satisfaction of heaven?
10037{_}2635{_}Not that we shall meet our friends, as so many seem to think, but that we shall awake in _his_ likeness and see _his_ face.
10037{_}2636{_}We shall be 'together,'--we have that comfort given us, but it will be 'together with the Lord.'
10037{_}2637{_}He is to be the centre of attraction and delight always.
10037{_}2638{_}What an unfathomable mystery it must be to the angels that he is not so with us now!"
10037{_}2639{_}Evadne took a long, yearning look at the dear face, as if she would imprint it upon her memory forever.
10037{_}2640{_}"He _is_ with you," she said softly.
10037{_}2641{_}"_You_ will never be a puzzle to the angels."
10037{_}2642{_}*       *       *       *       *  The time of her stay in Vernon drew near its close, and on the last day but one she went to say good-bye to Penelope Riggs.
10037{_}2643{_}She found her sitting alone in the house, her mother having taken a fancy to have a sun bath.
10037{_}2644{_}Her right hand was doubled up and she was rubbing it slowly up and down the palm of her left while she sang softly.
10037{_}2645{_}"Why, Penelope, what are you doing?"
10037{_}2646{_}cried Evadne in amaze.
10037{_}2647{_}"Polishin', child.
10037{_}2648{_}I learnt it long ago.
10037{_}2649{_}One day I was that wore out I wouldn't have cared if the sky had fallen,--things had been goin' crooked, an' Mother hadn't slept well for a fortnight, an' I was that narvous an' tuckered out I thought I'd fly to pieces.
10037{_}2650{_}There's an old hymn Mother's dredful fond of,--I don't remember how it goes now, but there's one line she keeps repeatin' over an' over till I feel ready to jump.
10037{_}2651{_}It's this,--'What dyin' wurms we be.'
10037{_}2652{_}So, when she begun her wurm song that mornin' I just let fly.
10037{_}2653{_}'Ef I _am_ a wurm,' sez I, 'I ain't goin' ter be allers lookin' to see myself squirm!'
10037{_}2654{_}and with that I up and out of the house.
10037{_}2655{_}My head was that tight inside I felt if I didn't git out that minit somethin' would snap.
10037{_}2656{_}I went straight up to Mis' Everidge's.
10037{_}2657{_}She's one of the people you see who always lives on a hill, inside an' out.
10037{_}2658{_}When I got there I couldn't speak.
10037{_}2659{_}My heart's weak at the best of times an' the weather in there was pretty stormy.
10037{_}2660{_}I just dropped into the first chair an' she put her hands on my two shoulders an' sez she,--'You poor child!'
10037{_}2661{_}an' then she went away an' made me a syllabub."
10037{_}2662{_}"'Look on the bright side,' sez she in her cheery way when I had finished drinkin'."
10037{_}2663{_}"'Sakes alive, Mis' Everidge,' sez I, 'there isn't any bright side!'"
10037{_}2664{_}"'Then polish up the dark one,' sez she, ez quick ez a flash.
10037{_}2665{_}I've been tryin' to do it ever since."
10037{_}2666{_}"You dear Penelope!"
10037{_}2667{_}exclaimed Evadne, "I think you have!"
10037{_}2668{_}"It's all a wale, child, a wale o' tears," old Mrs. Riggs complained as she bade her good-bye in the porch, but when she reached the turn in the road she heard Penelope singing,--    "Thy way, not mine, O Lord,     However dark it be!
10037{_}2669{_}Lead me by Thine own hand;     Choose out my path for me.
10037{_}2670{_}I dare not choose my lot,     I would not if I might;   Choose Thou for me, My God,     So shall I walk aright."
10037{_}2671{_}and Evadne knew that in the brave heart the voice of Christ had made the storm a calm.
10037{_}2672{_}"You dear Aunt Marthe!
10037{_}2673{_}How am I ever going to thank you for all you have been to me; and what shall I do without you?"
10037{_}2674{_}Evadne spoke the words wistfully.
10037{_}2675{_}They were making the most of their last evening.
10037{_}2676{_}"Why, dear child, we can always be together in spirit.
10037{_}2677{_}'It is not distance in miles that separates people but distance in feeling.'
10037{_}2678{_}Emerson says,--'A man really lives where his thought is,' so you can be in Vernon and I in Marlborough,--each of us held close in the hush of God's love, which 'in its breadth is a girdle that encompasses the globe and a mantle that enwraps it.'"
10037{_}2679{_}Evadne caught Mrs. Everidge's face between her hands and kissed it reverently.
10037{_}2680{_}"I mean to devote my life to making other people happy, as you do, my saint," she said.
10037{_}2681{_}*       *       *       *       *  "Board!"
10037{_}2682{_}The conductor's cry of warning smote the air and the train passengers made a final bustle of preparation for a start.
